Alden Anderson, Speaker pro tern, of

He related to the committee of the cir

Grant, Green took Anderson into a pri

way."
Mr. Anderson did not "take them

James P. Brown, who, with Green,

was to the effect that Grant had pour
ed money into his lap in exchange for
experience, but he was not sure the
amount exceeded one thousand dollars.
He had used the money, he said, in
paying hotel bills and traveling ex
penses, the latter item including a trip
East to see Steve Elkins and others in
the interests of Grant's candidacy. He
did say, however, that he had learned
that ex-Secretary of State L. H. Brown
was seeking to intimidate Grant's
supporters in the Examiner by telling
them if they continued to vote for
Grant they would be debarred from
any participation in the patronage of
the administration.
A verbatim report of all of the pro
ceedings before the committee to-day
is printed in full in this issue for the

of the facts as they are brought^ out
by the investigators.
SAN FRANCISCO, THURSDAY, JANUARY 19, 1899.
DIRECT OFFER OF GOLD
BY MILTON J. GREEN
And he (Green) tried to persuade me that it was Mr. Perkins' wish that Mr. Grant be
elected United States Senator from Southern California, and he attempted to show me some
letters, but I paid no attention to him, and he went on and said that he was lending some as
sistance to candidates and would like to assist me in my canvass. I told Mr. Green, as near as
I can remember, or words to this effect, that he did not know me very well, but it was no use
to make that kind of a proposition to me; that when I could not go to Sacramento as
a free moral agent, to act as I thought right and just for the best interests of the people that
I went there to represent, I would not go at all; I was willing to be defeated.
And with that I started to leave the room, but in the meantime Mr. Green had been
fumbling at a drawer or something, and he stepped around in front of me and said: ''You
had better take this, anyhow." He had a handful of $20 gold pieces, but I walked past him
and out, and from that day to this I have had no further conversation with him.
[Part of the testimony of Alden Anderson before the investigating committee at Sacramento.]

"I have two objections to this charter,"
plainted Mr. Johnson. "One is that it
provides that the next election shall fall
in 1S9!), and that all subsequent elections
shall occur every two years. This brings
the elections in the odd-numbered years,
and our State law provides that registra
tion must be effected in even-numbered
years, and in this way voters will be prac
tically disfranchised. This matter is the
woof, the web and the warp of the whole
charter and in my opinion invalidates the
wthole document,
"Another objection I have is that all
officers appointed hold office during the
pleasure of the appointing power. This is
unconstitutional,'' and Mr. Johnson
pulled his lawbooks to prove it.
Assemblyman Valentine evidently did
not know how to explain to himself Mr.
Johnson's peculiarly antagonistic position
and he got up to say so.
"I don't Bee," said Mr. Valentine, "just
what all this talk is for of unconstitu
tionality. This Legislature can neither
amend nor curtail the charter. It is sim
ply our duty to puss and give it the seal
of the State. The people of San Francisco
elected to frame their own organic law,
and they have done so and have sub
mitted it to us in order that it may be
legalized, and I shall certainly vote to
give our San Francisco friends what they
want."
Assemblyman Works said that San Di
ego and his section of the State were for
the charter, and it would give him pleas
ure to vote for it.
Then Speaker pro tern. Anderson shut
off further debate by putting the ques
tion, and the rollcall developed just two
votes against itâ Johnson and Charles
Kenneally of San Francisco. Kenneally's
vote, however, was not cast to beat the
charter, but to win a wager of a half
dozen good cigars he had laid with
Charles L. Patton that he wowW voie
against it. Kenneally got his cigars, and
gave notice that he changed his vote from
no to aye, and there was something fur
ther on Patton when the charter crowd
finally got to the Golden Eagle. Upon mo
tion of Dibble of San Francisco the char
ter resolution was immediately dis
patched to the Senate, where it is said
Johnson will find no second to his opposi
tion.

QUARTERS OF CLERKS
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 18.â George Root, the newly
elected Clerk of the Supreme Court, is
to live in the lap of luxury during the
term of his office: if it can be arranged by
legislative enactment. A bill was intro
duced in the Assembly this morning by
Assemblyman Cobb or San Francisco
that appropriates $11,000 to be expended
in furnishing the court clerk's office with
metallic files, bookcases and all the other
furniture necessary to business and solid
comfort. The bill was at once referred to
the* Ways and Means Committee and it is
understood that it will be pushed through
to its final passage as rapidly as the con
gested state of business will permit.
Belshaw of Contra Cofsta also got in
good work for the Supreme Court em
ployes with a bill that raises one of the
six deputies to the rank of chief deputy
and his salary from JISOO per annum to
$2400 per annum. It also carries the regu
lar appropriation for the salaries of the
deputies and stenographers. It is being
considered by the Ways and Means Com
mittee.
ALL THOUGHTS ARE
NOW CENTERED ON
THE INVESTIGATION
Legislative Proceedings Go On in a Most
Listless Manner.
COMMITTEE AN EARNEST BODY OF MEN.
A Determination Shown to Get to the Bottom of the
Senatorial Scandals Without Fear or Favor
to Any of the Wrongdoers.
BY HENRY JAMES.
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA- |
MENTO, Jan. 18.â There is no wild ex- j
cltement in watching the progress of a ;
ballot for United States Senator when a
similar ballot has been caf t from two j
to four times daily with the certainty of j
the arrival of the noon hour. To-day
there were two ballots, perfunctory, list
less, with no defection, even the little j
candidates with their suggestion of sup- j
port appearing in the same strength. |
Everybody was glad when a motion to !
adjourn was made, and it was carried
without dissent. There was a general
feeling that no change would have been
recorded if the members staid in their ;
seats all day. They are waiting, for ;
what Ido not know. They, do not know ;
themselves, but. that a break is coming j
soon nobody seems to doubt. Perhaps a i
good deal depends upon the work of the |
investigation committee which is engaged j
in trying to ascertain all the truth about j
Speaker Wright.
Any observer of this committee would j
be. bound to regard it as a sincere and
earnest body of men, working without j
fear and with no intent of shielding. The I
members seem to have a clear idea as |
to their duties. They are evidently law- j
yers or close students of legal methods.
None of the questioning is irrelevant, and
if the attorney for a witness breaks in j
he is quickly convinced that while he
may advise he must not attempt to die-
This evening Speaker Wright is on the
stand. He came into the room this after
noon and announced that he was ready
for the ordeal, but the committee was
not ready for him. Green was wanted
first. Green had been on the stand in
the morning and refused to answer as to
the destination of certain moneys he had
sent to help the campaigns vl candi
dates.
W hile Wright was in the room I got
the closest look at him. The man is un
der a tremendous strain, and though ho
shows It in a drawn face and a nervous
manner, his bold front, studied though it
may be, shows him to be a person with
some courage. From the knowledge or
testimony already given, from his own
words, tnÂ« sworn evidence of unbiased
men, it seems to me there awaits him
inevitable ruin and disgrace. It is not
simply that he voted for Burns, but that
to ao so he violated pledges more sacred
than any political promiae, and accepted
money from a candidate whom he had
agreed to support and whom he did not
support. This element of ingratitude
leids a darker hue to the crime. A man
may take money for his vote, and tim
plv be corrupt, but to take the money
and not deliver the vote adds to the act
a despicable quality not to be condoned.
But when Green was wanted he was ab
sent and while a message was sent for
him James Brown was put on the stand.
Mr Brown has been an associate mana
ger with Green, but if the committee ex
tracted any information from him 1
failed to catch it. With a never-failing
regularity he affably failed to remember.
So far as the committee is concerned,
Brown is a bad witness, but his contin
ual "passing of the buck" to Green made
the information stored in the mind or the
latter assume a new value.
Meanwhile Assemblyman Raw had
come in and expressed a desire to make
a few remarks. He was told that later
he would have abundant opportunity.
Then Green came in and took the stand.
He was not loquacious. The questions he
had refused to answer in the morning he
still refused to answer. As to the fact
that money was used to help the fight of
various candidates Green makes no se
cret. He asserts that the money was not
wrongfully used and that in ten in
stances at least it went to assist candi
dates who did not know of it, and were
still in ignorance. Another question was
put which had to be added to the list he
declined to answer. It was in relation to
a sum of money sent to Mendocino
County. Green admitted freely that the
money had been sent, but said it had
gone to a gentleman not actively in poli
tics, and who this gentleman is he would
not state. Finally he was granted the
privilege of postponing his reply until to
morrow. The fact was brought out that
this particular money had neen carried by
Green"s typewriter, a lady. There was
an immediate disposition to summon her,
but the witness knocked out this plan by
quoting law to show her not a competent
witness. However, the committee is not
wholly satisfied, and there may yet be a
spbpena issued for her. If she gets here
and has no right to talk Attorney Bacon
will be on hand to remind her of it.
There is an evening session with
Wright on the stand. As he is the central
figure of the whole s*candal there ought to
be something in the nature of a climax,
at least a dramatic passage, but this let
ter cannot wait for it. He cannot cl.-ar
himself, for his own words have already
condemned him, and to deny would be
but to impugn his own veracity. That
his claim of having the Supreme Court
behind him in his change to Burns is
false has been clearly demonstrated,
while the declaration of non-belief in the
charges against Burns cannot be com
forting to the colonel, for a moral bill of
health from Wright would be about as
hard a blow as a man could receive. Just
as the matter stands nojj* and taking
chances on the verdict of ihe committee.
I would advise Burns to get Wright to
denounce him. This would be good poll
tics.
Senator Boyce has joined the ranks of
those who dislike newspaper men. His
PRICE FIVE CENTS.
resolution, Introduced to-day, will tend to
make this feeling mutual. He wants it
considered in executive session, but the
scheme failed of unanimous support. Sen
ators Dickinson, Braunhart and Short
ridge were not impressed with the idea,
and before Boyce gets through monkeying
with the buzzsaw he will have fewer lin
gers with which to point with pride to<2its
statesmanship.
In writing of men and measures in
times when politics is hot one may ex
poct occasionally to give offense, and may
even be led into error. "When the latter
experience comes to me I am ready upon
finding it out to say so. The other
day I was given to understand that Frank
M .Stone wa.3 here in tl.e interests of
Burns, and would not only spend
money, but was ready to spend
it for newspaper influence, and r~ady
to talk J to me. This would have
touched the soreness, the pr'de of
any writer, and in this columr. 1 re
sented it. I do not believe that Stone
ever sent any message of any k'mc to
me. and I never sent any to him, al
though both of us were sincere for the
time in the contrary opinions. That in
cident, paving the way to wrath. I made
some other statements concerning Stone,
thinking they were upon good authority,
which does not appear to have been the
fact, I was told that he had won Grant's
confidence, assuming to be friendly, and
to be able to control certain votes. I
learn by inquiry that he has been openly
for Burns ever since Burns entered the
field, and that as to one man he was
charged with attempting to control he
has not even a speaking acquaintance,
nor has he attempted to control any of
them. His friendship for Grant has not
been political. The statement that the
cost of the excursion with young Grant
had been paid by Mr. Grant I find to be
incorrect. The expenses were defrayed by
Mr. Stone himself, and later D. S. Grant
insisted on assuming a share which, de
spite protests, he paid to Mr. Stf>ne. Why
I touched upon such personal matters is
clear, but I intend to be fair always, for
if a writer is not fair his contributions
lose value. Therefore I have gone into
so extended an explanation.
As I close word comes from the Capitol
that Wright has confessed to having re
ceived $1650 from Grant's manager, as
charged in The Call. Having done this
he uncorked the vials of his indignation
for the benefit of an employe of this pa
per who had been the chief instrument
in exposing him. The way he called
names is said to have been little less than
picturesque. Probably this line of de
fense was suggested by Wright's attor
ney. Devlin. This lawyer Is one of the lot
who conceived the plan of getting the
counties of the State to cinch the State
for a lot of back taxes. It was a great
scheme, too, r.nd would have netted the
lawyers more than a half million, while
the counties would have got that which
is vulgarly and expressively known as
soup. To "The Call fell the duty of ex
posing this project also, and doubtless
the disappointed attorneys, prospecting
in the pockets where the fees are not,
are conscious of a feeling of pain. If this
is the case they ought to say so and not
delegate the duty to a Wright, who has
troubles of his own.
The ethics of politics axe . peculiar. I
have heard nobody deny that. money can
be legitimately expended by a candidate,
but that this money should be accepted
from a man to whom the candidate will be
bound after election is a proposition
which finds no defenders.
DOCKERY'S BILL TO
BE AMENDED
CALL HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 18.â The Dockery bill, which
has for its object the creation of a State
Food Inspection Commission, will prob
ably be withdrawn by Its promoters or so
amended that instead of creating a new
commission it makes the proposed bursau
one of the departments of the State Board
of Health.
Dockery's bill has been pretty generally
and favorably discussed by the papers of
the State since it was introduced into the
Assembly in the early days of the session
and has the goodwill of most of the legis
lators, but Mr. Dockery has come to the
conclusion that just as much good can
be accomplished and with less cost to the
State by making it a department under
the State Board of Health, rather than a
commission by itself, and intends to so
amend it. It is at present in the hands of
the Judiciary Committee.

RELIEVE HIS CONSTITUENCY
CALL HEADQUARTERS. SACRA
MENTO, Jan. 18.â Grove t,. Johnson came
to tho front In the Assembly with still
another of his pet "expenditure bills."
This time Mr. Johnson wants to spend
$15,000 of the State's good money in fur
nishing the State library, one of the best
furnished departments under the Capitol
roof. His bill provides that the money
shall be spent in buyinK metallic desks
and files and ornamental stairways and
"other furniture that may be needed";
it also provides that it shall not be neces
sary to contract for the things bought,
but that they may be purchased out of
hand. Mr. Johnson's furniture bill is in
line with that other one he introduced
the other day which provides for the
cleaning o* the granite on the Capitol.
Both are evidently measures for the re
lief of an underfed constituency. The
last one is referred to the Committee oo
Ways and Means.2

AD VERTISEMEN TS.
Thousands of men in lonely homes call
up the pictures of the loved and lost ones,
who might still be happy husbands with
healthy babies prattling at their knees, if
they had only been a little more observant
and thoughtful. When a woman's com-
plexion gets pale and sallow and her eyes
look heavy; when she is evidently nervous
and despondent, and complains of pains
and aches and dragging down and burning
sensations; a wise man should know that
she is suffering from weakness or disease in
a womanly way. The thoughtful man who
realizes this will at once advise his wife to
consult some eminent and skillful special-
ist. He will know that the average physi-
cian will insist upon the obnoxious exam-
inations and local treatment so embarrass-
ing to modest, sensitive women. He should
know that these ordeals are unnecessary.
Dr. R. V. Pierce is an eminent and skillful
specialist, who has been for thirty years
chief consulting physician to the Invalids'
Hotel and Surgical Institute, at Buffalo,
N. Y. During that time, with the assist-
ance of a staff of able physicians, he has
prescribed for many thousands of wom-
en. He is the inventor of a wonderful
medicine for the special weaknesses of
women, that cures in the privacy of the
home. It is known as Dr. Pierce s Favorite
Prescription. It makes the delicate and
important organs that bear the burdens of
maternity strong, healthy and vigorous. It
corrects all irregularities and stops all de-
bilitating drains. Medicine dealers sell it
and have nothing "just as good."
W. R. Malcolm. Esq.. of Knobel, Clay Co.,
Ark., writes: "My wife for perhaps four months
previous to the birth of our child took the ' Fa-
vorite Prescription.' This strengthened her en-
tire system, and child-birth, to her, was very
easy, being attended with little pain. Our baby
Ruth is 13 mouths old and she has never been
a day." \ l_

ON THE STAND
Howard E. Wright was sworn.
The chairmanâ You may state your

A. Howard E. Wright.

A. I am Speaker of the Assembly.

to the Assembly?
A. Fifty-first District.

A. Berkeley, California.
Q. Are you acquainted with one W. S.
Le.'ike?

A. For several years.

A. Slightly.
Q. Do you know that he is a candidate
for election to the office of United States
Senator before this Legislature?
A. I do.
Q. How long have you known that he
would be such a candidate?
A. About four or five months.
Q. How did you first learn that fact?
A. By seeing hla name mentioned in the
newspapers.
>< Have you had any conversation with

didacy?
A. Not much.

A. Yes, sir.

matter?
A. Yes. sir. Ono day in the Palace Ho
tel. I have been in the habit for several
yeara of meeting Mr. Green on the boat,
going to the city with him, and I met
him one day in the Palace Hotel, when he
nsked me to step around the corner and

duced me to him at that time, arid told

didate for United ' States Senator, and

sembly?
A. Yes, sir.
Q. Financially?
A. Yes.
Q. You may state to the committee the
time and occasion and the persons pres
ent.
A. In September, some time preceding
the primaries in Alameda County, I was
engaged in advancing my candidacy. On
account of the intense bitterness accom
panying the candidacy of several of the
local county officers the district, which
ordinarily would be in my favor, was in
danger, and I saw that In order to elect
delegates that would be favorable to mÂ»
I should have to make a strong fight in
the district. Frank C. Jordan was the
candidate for County Clerk. He came
from the Seventh Ward, and It was
against him that the intense bitter feel
ing was directed, and in order to succeed
I had to combine with him to win out
the delegation in the Seventh Ward. The
talk In regard to his candidacy and the
danger in regard to mine was well known
and was very well known to my friend,
Mr. Green, who lives in Oakland, and
was watching the campaign with a good
deal of interest. The question as to what
procedure to take was often discussed by
all of us who were interested in the out
come, and it was determined that in or
der to win the delegation at all a great
deal of hard work and the expenditure
of money would have to be had. One
morning In crossing on the boat I met
Mr. Green.
Q. This was in September, I understand?
A. Yes, sir; before the primaries. And,
as waa usual at our meetings, we dis
cussed politics, and he said: "Howard,
I sco that you are going to have a des
perate fight over there." I said: "les,
Milt; it Is in a pretty hard position. I
don't know whether we are going to win
out or not," because the Leckey ticket in
the Seventh Ward was opposed to ours
and they were very strong. "Well, ' he
said, "in case you need any assistance at
all to carry your Seventh Ward delega
tion why let me know and I will help
you." "Well," I said, "Milt, we are inti
mate friends, but what does that involve?
Does it involve the Senatorial question in
case of my election?" "Why," he said,
"not a particle." He said: "I am interest
ed very largely in the success of the Re
publican ticketâ the Republican Legisla
tive ticketâ and you and I are strong per
sonal friends. I am Interested doubly in
you, in that I want to see you In the first
place elected; in the second place I want
to see a Republican Legislature elected."
"Well," I said, "I don't Know whether I
could accept that assistance without com
promising myself." "Well," he said, "put
it on the extreme personal grounds be
tween you and me. Rob it of the Sena
torial situation altogether and leave it
there." "Well," I said. "I will think about
it." Three or four days later I saw him
and told him that I had thought it over
and that with the express agreement and
condition between us that it would not af
fect my vote in the Senatorial matter, if
he as my friend wished to help me out in
that campaign over there I would accept
his assistance, and I did and expended the
money throughout the district and ac
complished the success of our delegation
by winning tho Seventh Ward by thÂ«i nar
row margin of six votes.

BILLS TO HELP TIE
AGRICULTURAL DISTRICTS
CAI,Li HEADQUARTERS, SACRA
MENTO, Jan. IS.â The prediction of Sen
ator Langford, the father of the Senate,
that the session of 1599 would probably
result In more good to the agricultural
interests than any of tne recent previous
sessions bids fair to be realized. Already
bills are in relieving the district agricul
tural societies of the burden of debt that
has hampered their efficient working, and
now irrigation matters are coming in for
more than their usual meed of attention.
The problem of bringing water to the
naturally fruitful soil of the San Joaquin
Valley has already conjured up two bills
proposing the building of storage reser
voirs and the husbanding of water sup
plies and to-day Assemblyman Fair
weather of Fresno sent another to the
Irrigation Committee, constructed along
the same lines, but more comprehensive
In its scope than any that have yet been
considered.
Assemblymnn Falrweather s bill in
structs the Board of Auditors to the
Commissioner of Public Works to cause
surveys to be made uro-und the head
waters of all streams running through
arid lands or lands that would be bene
fited by irrigation, for the purpose of de
termining where storage reservoirs and
diverting dams for the diversion of flood
waters could be most advantageously
built.
The first surveys are to be made along
Ktngs River and Its tributaries In Fresno
County, and during the first two years
following the passage of the bill all the
streams and their tributaries flowing into
the San Joaquin Valley are to be sur
veyed. The sum of $10,000 Is appropri
ated to defray the cost of the survey
upon the condition that the officers of
the Geodetic Survey set aside a like
amount for the same purpose.

SPAIN READY
TO CEDE THE
EASTERN ISLANDS
The President's Commission to
the Philippines Will Go Out in
an Advisory Capacity.
> LONDON, Jan. 19.â The Spanish Cabinet, according to the Madrid
' correspondent of the Daily Mail, has decided to submit a bill to the
* Cortes sanctioning the cession of the Philippines before presenting the
[ peace treaty for ratification.
NEW YORK, Jan. 18.â A Washing
ton special to the Herald says: Presi
dent McKinley is expected to announce
in a few days the Instructions he has
given to the Schurman Commission,
which will investigate economic condi
tions existing in the Philippine Islands.
Mr. Schurman had a final conference
with the President this morning and is
making arrangements to leave San
Francisco, by the latter part of the
month. Professor Dean C. Worcester
of Ann Arbor University reached
Washington to-day and will confer
with the President to-morrow. It is
expected that he will sail with Presi
dent Schurman. Colonel Denby's work
with the War Investigation Commission
may prevent his departure with his
colleagues, but he will sail soon after
ward.
Upon the arrival of the commission
ers at Hongkong they will be met by
a Government transport and proceed in
it to Manila, where they will meet Ma
jor General Otis and Rear Admiral
Dewey. Under instructions given to
the commissioners they will make a
thorough investigation of the habits
and the economic and social conditions
of the Filipinos and will make a report
upon the character of government
which, in their opinion, is best suited
for them. The commission is entirely
advisory to the President and its re
port will be useful to him in outlining

CHAMBERLAIN HOPEFUL
. FOR "OPEN-DOOR" POLICY
LONDON, Jan. 18.â Rt. Hon. Joseph
Chamberlain, Secretary of State for the
Colonies, speaking to-night at the ban
quet of the "Wolverhampton Chamber
of Commerce, reviewed at great length
the various threatening foreign ques
tions confronting the Government.
In connection with the withdrawal of
the French demand for an extension of
the settlement at Shanghai, Mr. Cham
berlain congratulated his hearers that
Great Britain's opposition to the pro
posal had been supported by the rep
resentatives of the United States.
"That, I think," he said, "a significant
and noteworthy fact. Nay, I think and
hope it will become history. It shows
that circumstances are bringing about
the community of interests between
Anglo-Saxons, which in the future may
have far-reaching and beneficial re
sults."
Smith and his motion was carried as soon
as Speaker pro tern. Anderson could put
it to the house.
The two ballots taken to-day made no
change in the vote. It is the same an
yesterday and the clay before and as it
will be to-morrow, unless every indica
tion is false.
Grant polled his full voteâ 2Sâ on the first
ballot for the first time this week. As
semblyman Raw of El Dorado, who has
been .seriously indisposed, was again in
his seat, as was Chynoweth of Orange.
Speaker Wright was engaged at the meet
ing of the investigation committee and
could not be on hand to cast his dollar
marked vote for the Mexican candidate,
Burns as a consequence got but 25 of his
list of 26.
General Barnes polled his usual ten and
Bulla held on with the nine who have
stayed by him.
Knight, Scott, Estee, Paterson, Bard
and Rosenfeld got their customary two.
Democratic honors were divided, though
not evenly, between Stephen M. "White
and Marlon De Vries. White got 2.), De
Vriea 2.
On the second ballot Grant tallied one
vote short on account of Raw having
paired with Speaker Wright, refusing to
vote.
The Grant people have decided to stay
where they are until the end of the pres
ent week, anyway, but they are casting
about for some one to whom they may
throw their strength with no danger of
electing Burns by doing so. It was
feared by some of them that Chynoweth
would bolt the camp when the vote came
on this morning, but he is lined up as
solidly as ever and with every intention
of staying with the crowd until it is con
vinced its cause is hopeless.

Three Koads to Consolidate.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 18.â The Senate
Committee on Corporations decided this
evening to recommend for passage the
bill which will enable the consolidation of
the Atlantic and Pacific, the Southern
California and the San Francisco and
San Joaquin Valley roads under one cor
porate name. The object of bringing
about the consolidation is to enable the
three roads to be operated as one, with
one set of officers and one set of accounts,
thereby effecting a great saving.
Advances made on furniture and pianos, with
or without removal. J. Noonan. 1017-1023 Mission.
ELECTRICITY BENEFICIAL
TO BISHOP NEWMAN
NEW YORK, Jan. IS. âBishop John P.
Newman of the Methodist Episcopal
Church of California, after a month's
treatment with an electric current, is in a
fair way to complete recovery from an
injury received many years ago. Bishop
Newman said to-day:
"I came East a month ago for medical
treatment and visited a physician who
makes a specialty of electricity. He dis
covered a lesion at the base of the spine,
due to my accident in California, and
which I had entirely overlooked. Since
then I have had diffusive system elec
tricity applied to me for one and two
hours at a time several days a week. I
am almost completely well again and can
walk excellently."

WILL SUPERINTEND THE
PHILIPPINE POSTOFFICES
WASHINGTON, Jan. 18.â F. W.
Vaille of Oregon, who, on the acquisi
tion of the Philippine Islands, was dis
patched to Manila as United States pos
tal agcrit, to be In charge there, will
shortly be appointed director of posts
for that archipelago, with functions
similar to those of an assistant post
master here. He will have complete
control, under the supervision of Post
master General Charles Emory Smith
of the administration of the postal af
fairs of those islands.
Mr. Vaille has been in the service as
assistant superintendent of the railway
mail service at Portland, Or., for nine
years. He Is a native of Massachu
setts. As postal agent he has made a
strong record in organizing and extend
ing a postal scheme there. It had been
suggested to send a commission of ex
perts to the Philippines, with a view of
making recommendations for the ser
vice, but the Postmaster General, espe
cially in view of the small number of
offices, decided against that action as
unnecessary, and will directly entrust
the executive responsibility to Mr.
Veille. The latter will make constant
reports, advising the administration
here of the postal situation in that por
tion of the east.

GERMANY INVITES
WAR OVER SAMOA
Defiance of America and
England.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
AUCKLAND, N. Z., Jan. IS.â Further
advices from Samoa are to the effect
that after Chief Justice Chambers had
declared Malietoa King the Consuls of
the United States and Great Britain
and the captains of the German war
ship Falke and the British warship
Porpoise met the German Consul, who
refused to recognize Malietoa and de
clined to co-operate in the disposal of
the Samoans, who thereupon assembled
in large numbers at Mulinuu, armed
themselves and surrounded the mu
nicipality. Malietoa and Tamasese
mustered about 2000 men, well armed,
but supplied with defective ammuni
tion. The British and American Con
suls endeavored to avert hostilities, but
they were commenced on January 1.
Malietoa and Tamasese fought brave
ly, but 500 of their followers were cap
tured. Then, disheartened and out
numbered, the two chiefs sought refuge
on the British warship, and their fol
lowers sought protection under the
guns of the Porpoise. Mataafa's loss
was sixty-one .men killed and wounded,
and Malietoa lost twelve men killed and
wounded.
The followers of Mataafa looted and
burned Apia,
It transpires that Dr. Raffel. assisted
by the German Consul, closed the Su
preme Court, the two declaring: that
the power was vested in them. The
British and American Consuls then
appealed to the captain of the British
ship Porpoise, who landed bluejackets,
and Chief Justice Chambers, under
their guard, took his seat. Mataafa
held aloof from these last develop
ments.
LONDON, Jan. 19.â The Times, com
menting on the trouble in Samoa, says
that the only satisfactory feature ap
pears to have been the cordial co
operation of the British and American
authorities.

SAN JOSE, Jan. IS.â The Councitmen
failed to appear at the meeting: this
afternoon and consequently nothing
was done toward selecting a City
Treasurer. Members of the Council en
deavored to get Mayor Martin into a
conference to-day but failed. A special
meeting is called for to-morrow after
noon, when, Martin says, he will re
name Colombet. The treasury remains
closed and interest in the deadlock Is
increasing. There is an unusually large
number of applicants for the treasurer
ship.
Calvin E. Carpenter Dead.
NAPA, Jan. 18.â Calvin E. Carpenter.
a native of New York, aged 77 years,
died to-day. Mr. Carpenter came to
California in 1849. He prospected and
mined in El Dorado and Tuba Counties.
Twenty years ago he settled in Foss
Valley, Napa County. He studied law
and was an intellectual and mechanical
genius in his prime. He has 'oeen total
ly blind for many years. With a cane
he could find his way in town and coun
try.

GIVEN A BREATH
OF FRESH AIR
For a Brief Hour the Woman Ac
cused of Conspiracy to Mur
der* Enjoys Freedom.
SUISUN, Jan. 18.â Mrs. Dickinson [
was given a breath of fresh air this j
afternoon and for fully an hour the
woman, who Is accused of having been j
the head of the conspiracy that resulted j
in the murder of old Dan Wilson, was
free from the restraint of prison bars
and vigilant Deputy Sheriffs. Attorney !
Lamont visited the Sheriff's office J
shortly after 2 o'clock and requested an i
interview with his client. The at
| torney has a well-defined aversion to
1 the gloomy old jail, and as there was
no private room in the Courthouse to |
which they could retire, he suggested
that he be allowed to take the prisoner
to his office, which is just across the
road. His suggestion was favorably j
received and Mrs. Dickinson was j
escorted to the office of her lawyer for j
a consultation. She appeared greatly J
downcast and the assurances of L.a- I
mont did not appear to have a very I
cheering effect, for when she returned J
to the Jail she sorrowfully expressed
the hope that "no other innocent per- !
son will ever have to endure what I am j
going through."
Later in the day Mrs. Dickinson sent
a request to the Sheriff's office for the
daily papers, adding the proviso that 'â 
she did not care for them if the articles j
relating to the Wilson case were cut ]
out. As nothing bearing on the case j
is allowed in the jail her request was
not granted.
District Attorney Devlin is busily
engaged in preparing for the prelimi
nary examination, which is set for next
Tuesday. He has carefully gone over
the evidence given at the Coroner's
inquest and the statements of wit
nesses whose testimony is relied upon t
to hold the three prisoners in the
County Jail for trial in the Superior
Court. Much of the evidence has no j
value when taken separately and some j
of the witnesses are far from being j
wholly satisfactory to the prosecution, j
but when taken as a whole the evidence j
is believed to be amply sufficient to !
warrant the prisoners being held.
Detective Stranahan, who collected '
the major part of the testimony against j
Mrs. Dickinson and Mrs. Wilson and '
Miller, is one of the witnesses whose
veracity is regarded as open to criti- i
cism and the amateur sleuth will not i
be called upon to take the witness !
stand. The fruits of his efforts, how- i
ever, are capable of corroboraion to a
great extent, so that the absence of the
man who was one of the chief figures
in the prosecution may not materially
weaken the State's case.
The main features or facts upon
which the prosecution will build its
case, so far as known, consist of the
alleged secret visit of Miller to the
Wilson ranch on the evening of the
murder; the peculiar actions of Mrs.
Dickinson and Mrs. Wilson, who, the
prosecution will endeavor to prove, dis

played guilty restlessness while wait
ing to hoar the report of the gun which
was the death knell of old Dan Wilson,
and who were in bed and fully dressed
when notified of the assassination a few
hours later, and the incriminating re
mark and admissions alleged to have
been made by all three of the accused
prisoners at various times during the
two years which have elapsed since the
consummation of the deed.
This is the case of the prosecution in
outline, so far as known at this time,
but it is intimated that when the proper
time arrives a surprise of the sensa
tional sort will be sprung which will
leave no doubt of the guilt of the ac
cused and render their conviction ab
solutely certain.
So far as the defense is concerned, it
depends wholly upon the testimony of
the Sheehan girls, Maggie and Kate, of
Vallejo. If they adhere to the story
they have heretofore told, when placed
on the witness stand to give their testi
mony under oath, they may do a great
deal in proving the alibi claimed by
Miller, providing their testimony is not
impeached by the prosecution. There
are many who doubt the veracity of the
Sheehan girls and the District Attorney
may controvert the effect of any tes
timony favorable to the defense they
may give my producing witnesses to
show they are not worthy of belief.
With the exception of Mrs. Dickin
son's somewhat brief outing, the threa
prisoners were left to their reflections
undisturbed to-day, in pursuance of
the policy adopted by Under Sheriff
Robinson several days ago. No visit
ors were admitted to the jail. The
enforced solitude is evidently wearing
on the alleged slayers of the old
rancher and they would gladly wel
come a return to the former plan of
being allowed to hold daily receptions.
Miller mantains his air of confidence to
a remarkable degree, but the women
make no attempt to conceal their ap
prehension. Sleepless nights, combined
with prison fare, which they scarcely
touch, are beginning to undermine
their health and the least thing is liable
to set them trembling with nervous
fears. They may not break down, but
should they collapse at any time no one
here would be in the least surprised.
They are not the same women physic
ally or mentally they were when first
placed behind the bars a week ago to
day.
3IOTJRNING IN CAXAVKRAS.
ANGELS CAMP, Jan. 18.â John J.
Snyder, District Attorney of Calaveras
County, died to-day at his home at San
Andreas. Mr. Snyder was born and
educated in this county. At Murphys
he worked at mining, earning sufficient
money to educate himself. He was a
man of sterling integrity.
Mr. Snyder was a prominent Mason
and Odd Fellow, 34 years of age. He
leaves a widow and child.

THE CALLS NEWS SERVICE.
V' ESTERDAY The Call gave its readers a
double proof of the superiority of its news ser
vice over that of all its competitors combined.
It was the only papef in the city to publish a verbatim
report of the proceedings of the investigating com
mittee at Sacramento, and was also the only paper
to publish the cablegram from Auckland announcing
the i inbreak of war in the Samoan Islands.
These two items constituted the most important
events, local or foreign, of the day. No issue of do
ic affairs is so interesting or so noteworthy as
that involved in the hearing of the Senatorial scandal
at Sacramento. On that subject the people ought to
know the whole truth, and The Call gave it, while
the ether papers made a garbled digest, which fur
nished but an inadequate treatment of the subject.
The outbreak of the natives of the Samoan Islands
i;-. fraught with the danger of involving the United
States. Great Britain and Germany in the controversy,
for t!:e administration of the islands is under their
rol. The islands hold a strategic position in
the Southern Pacific, and the interests of the three
powers in them arc great. Therefore the news of the
outbreak is a matter of no small concern to the world
Oi course cablegrams and full stenographic reports
of important proceedings cost money. It would be
cheaper to fill ? newspaper with fake interviews with
the : of China or the Pope, and make a freak
affairs, but The Call is not that kind of
â  It docs no; fake, nor docs it spare money for
news. Those who wish to be posted must i
The Call.

thcr the proposal of Speaker Wright to ex
clude representatives of The Call from the floor of
Hie Assembly has gone over or gone under is not
clear, but it is certainly gone.
Caesar had his Brutus, Ulysses Grant has had his
Green, and Dan Burns may profit by the example.
THE POLITICAL GRUBSTAKE-
IF it were not serious it would be amusing to wit
ness the Democratic horror at The Call's expo
sure of the system of political grubstaking fol
lowed by candidates for the United States Senate.
Under that system love and affection have no place,
but go to the limbo of obsolete things along with
party loyalty and State patriotism. It recognizes
money as the only merit in a candidate. A man may
have the accomplishments of the Admirable Crichton,
the patriotism of Washington, the profundity of Web
ster and the eloquence of Clay, but these qualifica
tions for the Senatorship cut no figure against an
ignorant and accidentally rich man who can grub
stake candidates for the Legislature and thereby se
cure their votes when elected.
Perhaps in the beginning this practice was not con
sidered reprehensible. When the late Senator Hearst
desired to acquire this great public honor he entered
upon an extensive grubstaking enterprise. Demo
cratic candidates whom he had not seen personally
received his check and a request to invest it in secur
ing those ballots declared by Whittier to â
" â¢ * â¢ fall as light as snowflakea on the sod.
But execute the freeman's will
As lightning does the will of God."
Several gentlemen returned these checks, and if
they succeeded in getting elected were viewed with
alarm, while those who kept the checks and were
elected were pointed to with pride. This system was
the cause of the enacti..ent of the purity of elections
law. Under it a legislative candidate appoints a
purity committee which audits his election expenses
and files the account under oath.
The revelations current at Sacramento make it ap
parent that some candidates needed an impurity com
mittee.
In the case of Speaker Wrong it is not yet estab
lished that his campaign accounts were audited at all
or that the members of his purity committee ever
made oath to the required statement.
One fact in his case stands out in forcible relief.
According to his own statement he paid an opponent
for the nomination to withdraw in his favor, and then
appointed the same man a member of his purity com
mittee! This money paid for the right of way to the
nomination was furnished from the exchequer of a
candidate for the United States Senate, and, though
contributed from that source as part of the Speaker's
political expenses, the gentleman to whom it was paid
did not find it among the audited accounts of the can
didate!
Since the introduction of the political grubstake the
system seems to have undergone changes and to have
been applied to the purchase of right of way in the
party convention as well as to the purpose of fighting
the common enemy, by opening the eyes of voters to
the virtues of the grubstaked candidate.
The student of statutes may get a list of suggestions
for amendment of the purity of elections law, for the
enforcement of the responsibilities of a purity com
mittee, and for getting purity and impurity far enough
apart so that one can be distinguished from the other.
As far as known this grubstake is a Democratic in
vention that has been pirated by certain Republican
politicians, though it is by no means sure that the
rightful owner of the invention will bring action for
d-mages.
THE COYOTE SCOLP STEAL.
A BILL has been introducted into the Legisla
ture appropriating $287,000, with which to
liquidate the coyote scalp claims incurred under
the statute repealed four years ago. The measure
provides that payment shall be made by the State
Treasurer during the next two fiscal years, so that
the entire burden will not fall in any one year. It is
enacted that the State Board of Examiners shall ex
amine all the claims and pay only those which seem
to it just and proper.
While, of course, it is absolutely necessary that the
coyote claims should be thoroughly investigated, we
do not think the power to make the inquiry should
be conferred upon the State Board of Examiners.
That body consists of the Governor, Secretary of
State and Attorney General, three officials who have
plenty to do without this additional duty. In the na
ture of things they cannot give to the subject the at
tention it deserves, which is a serious matter, since
not a single coyote claim should be paid until some
body is thoroughly satisfied that it is genuine.
It is notorious that during the time the statute re
ferred to was in force coyote farming in certain por
tions of the State was a profitable industry. It has
been charged on excellent authority that many agri
culturists in the mountain counties added coyote
scalps to their regular output and made a great deal
of money thereby. It is also well known that large
numbers of coyotes were killed in Arizona and
Nevada and their scalps brought into this State.
There was at one time suftkient evidence to establish
the fact that bales of coyote scalps were authenti
cated in several different counties, being shipped from
one to the other by collusive County Clerks.
Moreover, these claims have all now taken on a
speculative character. They have been purchased at
enormous discounts from the original holders and
have fallen into the hands of banks and capitalists.
The biennial attempt to pay them, therefore, has be
come a regular raid upon the treasury. Two years
ago money was openly used in the lobby to pass a
bill appropriating money to pay them, and consid
erable scandal resulted from charges preferred against
one man of bribing a member of the Legislature.
The Legislature owes it to itself to see that this
bill does not pass until ample provision has been
made for a thorough investigation of the claims and
the establishment of their genuineness. Any other
course cannot fail to result in the actual looting of
the treasury.
A GROSS INJUSTICE.
UNDER the decision of the Commissioner of In
ternal Revenue, that the war revenue act pro
hibits tobacco dealers from selling in broken
packages, or small quantities, a wrong will be done
to an industrious portion of the people which
amounts to a gross injustice â one so gross, indeed,
that it threatens to deprive many families of their
means of subsistence and reduce them to penury.
The circumstances are these: In San Francisco
and in every American city of considerable popula
tion there are a number of persons who carry on
the industry of cigar-making in a small way. These
Workers are not able to purchase tobacco in large
quantities, for the limited extent of their trade and
their means do not permit it. Their industry, there
fore, depends upon their ability to obtain tobacco in
small quantities as they require it. and as the new
law. according to the decision of the Commissioner,
forbids the sale in that way, the industry is virtually
suppressed by an act of Congress.
The evil is all the more offensive because the eco
! nomic tendencies of the age are already heavily han
; dicanping the small producer or manufacturer in
j competition with the giant corporations and trusts
j which control so much capital. The large manufac
j turers of cigars have a hundred advantages over the
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 19, 1899.
smaller cigar-makers by reason of the very nature of
our economic system. It is, therefore, a most griev
ous wrong that the law should step in and give such
further advantage to the big factories as will suppress
the independent workers altogether.
It is stated by the Commissioner in his decision, as
an explanation of the adoption of the new rule, that
'"increased rates of tax on tobacco products enforces
a more careful supervision over the sale and disposi
tion of leaf tobacco." As an explanation that may be
ample, but as a justification it is worth nothing. The
United States are not so poor that they havf4o im
pose taxes that literally crush out the industries of
the people. There are many ways in which the needed
revenues of the Government may be raised without
resorting to such exactions upon small industries,
and if the tobacco tax cannot be collected under the
new law with as little injustice as under the old it
would be better to return to the former rate.
The issue is one which cannot be postponed. The
wrong that has been done should be impressed upon
Congress at the present session. As the matter
stands, if Congress does not take some action to
amend the law and do justice to the small dealers,
there will be some suspicion that the big cigar fac
tories have cunningly devised this scheme to drive
their small competitors out of business and compel
them to seek employment in the big factories as
operatives, and possibly at lower wages than the pres
ent operatives are earning.
The cigar-makers should not be left to fight the
battle alone. The issue is one that concerns the
whole community. It is estimated there are upward
of 2000 cigar-makers in this city alone, and that there
are dependent upon them something like three times
that number. The ruin of the industry out of which
is derived the maintenance of that number of persons
would be a serious affair under any circumstances,
even if brought about by -the irresistible development
of forces of economic change, but when done by an
act of legislation it is an injustice which should not
tolerated a day after its effects have been noted.
THE ELECTION OF SENATORS.
WITH the return of the season of Senatorial
elections there has come a renewed demand on
the part of intelligent and patriotic citizens
for an amendment to the constitution providing that
such elections shall be held by direct vote of the peo
ple instead of by Legislatures.
The demand is not only renewed, but is augmented
in force. At each successive period the scandal of
Senatorial struggles becomes worse and the need of
change more distinctly perceived. The advocates of
the desired reform increase in numbers and in the
energy of their efforts to bring it about. Sooner or
later such an amendment must be provided in the
general interests of the country, and the sooner it is
adopted the better.
Bad as are the open and notorious evils that lie on
the surface of Senatorial struggles in the Legisla
tures, these are by no means the greatest wrongs pro
duced by the present system of election. Back of
them are other offenses which affect all the legislation
of the session at which such elections are held, and
materially vitiate and corrupt the whole political de
velopment of the State.
The scandals that are more readily perceived and
exposed are those of bribery, either by defraying the
election expenses of a candidate for the Legislature
or by the distribution of money afterward. Such
ofienses are serious enough, since they enable cor
rupt men to advance to high office and prevent hon
esty and merit from attaining the honors they de
serve, but their evil terminates, however, with the
election of the vicious candidate, while there are
other offenses that go further.
Corruption, in working its snaky way to high
office, rarely resorts to the purchase of votes by
money. . It uses such bribes only for the lower grade
of rascals who are in politics solely for boodle. It has
more powerful inducements or compulsions to bring
to bear upon the better grade of legislators, and it is
by these it inflicts the greater, more far-reaching and
more enduring wrongs upon the State and the people.
One of these indirect means of winning votes is
that of patronage. For the purpose of promoting
the candidacy of a corrupt man his allies in office dis
tribute the patronage of the State among those who
will support him. Men who are utterly unfit to be
intrusted with public service, and often those who
are notoriously vicious and dishonest, are given im
portant positions because they have influence with
some member of the Legislature and can control his
vote for Senator. Frequently new offices are created
for the purpose of increasing this patronage, and the
taxpayers are thus burdened not only with incom
petent and base office-holders, but with a considerable
number who render no service whatever.
Another means is that of offering to furnish votes
for certain measures on condition that the advocates
of the measures will vote for the Senator to whom the
offered votes are pledged. Such combinations are so
frequently made it often happens that the legislation
of a whoje session is determined by the wire-pulling
and log-rolling of the Senatorial contest.
The evil? of these elections are, in fact, so far
reaching they affect the interests of every class of
people and legislation of all kinds. Such offenses, in
creasing with the years, have now become intolerable.
It is time to put an end to them. Under the present
system, to attain a Scnatorship a candidate must
either have great wealth or be backed by corporations
that supply him with wealth. Let us revive the
spirit of the constitution by amending the letter, and
once more make the avenues to the Senate open to
integrity, ability and patriotism, so that the welfare of
the State may be preserved and "corruption win not
more than honesty."
When a man is detected leaving a ship and wrapped
ir forty yards of silk there is almost warrant for be
lief that he is engaged in smuggling. Possibly, how
ever, the Mallaby jury was prejudiced against cir
cumstantial evidence.
One way to weaken that depressing deadlock would
be for some of the little candidates to drop out. The
difficulty, however, would be to find any candidate
who would consider himself in this classification.
Noble efforts to save the charter continue. As
the charter is not in danger, and really has no op
position, these efforts may be regarded as spectacular
rather than useful.
The best case of well-directed economy afforded by
any Legislature this winter is that of Tennessee. The
solons refused to vote money to print the Governor's
message.
A person who writes an anonymous letter to
charge another person with murder will excuse the
public if it fail to manifest signs of respect.
Even if Spain were to recognize the belligerency of
the Filipinos the moral effect would be small. Spain
once thought herself belligerent.
He is a very busy statesman who docs not find
time to reach for The Call as soon as it gets to the
Capitol.

spltework on the part of a disgruntled

I rllous letter without having first verified

nel Smith's feelings in the matter will
recognize at once that he never declared

i What he did say was that if the Govern
ment thought that tho services of the reg
iment were required in Manila he, as well

vices. These are the exact feelings of

gusted with the whole outfit. The ra
tions are good and their quarters the
j best in Manila. In my quarters there was
| room for thirty more men. Each man had
J a cot and a mosquito net attached there-
I to.
"In regard to Colonpl Smith's political
: aspirations I can safely say that he has
I none, and it is not redounding to his
[financial benefit to remain where he is.
I He could make more money by returning
i to his law practice in this city, which
i ha? suffered on account of his absence.
"I consider this unwarranted attack
I upon a man who has done so much for

Colonel Smith ha? been unremitting in

No Stop, No Charter.
The following letter, received yesterday
by the secretary of the Railroad Commis
sioners in this city, explains itself:
LOS ANGELES, Jan. 15, 1599.
To the Railroad Commissioners, State of Cali
fornia: I have a complaint to make against
the Southern Pacific Railroad Company for vio
lation of the law of this State. On November
15, 1889, 1 flagged the southbound â  train at
Honby, due about 5:45 a. m., for Los Angeles,
and the engineer refused . to stop. Honby is a
Bag station on said road, located about thirty
seven miles north of Los Angeles.' It is always
the rule to stop at this station on flagging, .and
besides, having purchased a ticket at that
point, it was their duty to haul me back.
I made complaint at the offices In Los An
geles and was referred to the division super
intendent at Bakernfleld. I wrote to him In
forming him of the- same, and no attention
was paid to it. Therefore your petitioner prays
that the commissioner* investigate these
charges, and if proved to be true that the
Southern Pacific Railroad Company forfeit their
charter to the State. Very respectfully,
l-\ .T. PATTERSON',

NOTED iitEN AT THE TRAPS.
Tod Sloane and Johnny Coleman Will
Shoot a 100-Bird Race To-Day.
"I have made my fortune In the saddle,
but of all sports I think nothing is more
enjoyable than shooting," said Tod Sloan
last evening to his friend Johnny Cole
tnan, who, by the way, is a smasher at
the traps.
"Can you really shoot. Tod?" queried
Coloman in a rather astonished tone.
Sloan did not speak for fully five min
utes. He felt stung at being asked such
a stupid question, but finally he mustered
up sufficient courage to put one to Cole
man.
"Look here, Johnny, me boy, if you
have an idea that I can't shoot I'll just
bet you a champagne supper that I can
drop more birds any day before the traps
than you have ever been known to kill.
Coleman was staggered.
The presumption of the little man of
pig skin fame challenging him to a pig
eon shoot, and live birds at that, was
something unforeseen, unexpected, un
thought of: but Tod meant every word
he said, and Coleman had to accept the
challenge or de the laughing stock of the
gun shooting fraternity.
"Well, Tod. old boy. I guess I must ac
commodate ynu.notwithstandingthat you
are reported' to have bagged more grouse
than the Prince of "Wales on the Scot
tish moors in a day's sport." was the
smiling retort of Bookmaker Jack.
The two crackajacks then repaired to
the rooms of the Olywpie Gun Club, wher?
articles of agreement wore drawn up and
signed by the shooters to meet this after
noon at the shooting grounds of the
Olympic Gun Club and shoot at 100 bird?
each for the supper. Hurlingham rules
will govern the race and thp articles
ended up with the statement. "May the
best man win." Al dimming will act as
second for Sloan and Sloan will be es
qulted by Eddie Bald, the wheelman.
Referee and official scorers will be select
ed on the grounds. Coleman is a slight
favorite in tli>- betting, but it is though:
that the friends of Sloan will open up
their sacks to-day.

A NOBMAL SCHOOL PLAN.
Pupils Willing to Contribute to a
Fund to Maintain It.
A meeting of the Normal School stu
dents was held yesterday afternoon. The
young ladies agreed upon a plan of pay-
Ing their own tuition to the school, and
will submit their determination to the
Board of Education at its next meeting.
Each pupil proposes to contribute $5 per
month to the general school fund, and in
tend by so doing to break the econom
ical objection which has been brought to
bear against the existence of the institu
tion. By placing the money in the gen
eral school fund it cannot be construed
as being an appropriation to maintain an
independent school.
A petition is being circulated and has
already gained the signatures of over 1000
residents of this city urging that the Nor
mal School be continued until the end of
June. It sets forth that the pupils en
tered the school at the beginning of the
term with the intention of graduating,
and should not be robbed of this opor
tunitv.
A mass-meeting will be held in Depart
ment 6 of the Superior Court in the City
Hall for the purpose of protesting against
the action of the School Board. The par
ents of the pupils and the public are in
vited to be present.

ANOTHER WAR ON THE
EDUCATIONAL SOCIETY
Chinese Slave Owners Begin - Fight
Similar to the Cne Lost a
Year Ago.
The trial of Ong Foon, arrested on the
charges of vagrancy and attempted ex
tortion and blackmail, which comes he
fore Judge Mogan this afternoon, is one
in which there is much interest in China
town.
For several years the Chinese Society
for English Education has been a strong
factor in the suppression of slave dealing
and illicit immigration, and the result has
been that the slave dealers' association
has used every means in its power to
break up the society.
About a year ago these attacks on the
society were fully exposed in The Call,
and it was then shown that thousands of
dollars had been raised among the keep
ers and inmates of the houses of ill fame
in Chinatown for the purpose of suborn
ing witnesses in order to break up the
Educational Society.
Ong Foon is an active member of the
Educational Society and has been instru
mental in numerous cases in sending
back to China women who were brought
here as slaves. The slave dealers and
owners have had him arrested on the
charges set forth above and expect to
strike through him the first blow of an
other attack on the society. Foon claims
to be able to fully disprove all the
charges against him. and the society is
ready to take immediate action for its
protection as soon as the case is decided.
At the time of the former attack it was
conclusively shown by the police that the
Educational Society w r as the only society
in Chinatown which never gave trouble,
and durir.pr the existence of the organiza
tion no member had bctn arrested on any
charge, and at no time of the day or night
had the police ever found the door of tha
society's rooms locked.
An investigation into the conditions
surrounding the present fight shows that
another fund has been raised by the slave
dealers, but the society expresses confi
dence in its ability to meet all charges
and attacks that may be made upon it.

AN UNKNOWN SUICIDE.
Believed to Be Mrs. Kaelber of Mar
tinez, Contra Costa County.
A woman about 35 years old, supposed
to be Mrs. Kaelber, committed suicide
last Tuesday night by taking half the
contents of a six-ounce bottle of carbolic
acid. She engaged a room last Monday
in the Carroll House, 124 Sixth street. She
represented that she was a stranger and
that she wanted employment. Mrs. Shee
han, the landlady, got her some washing
to do for yesterday, but when the lodger
was called she failed to respond. The
door of her room was tightly locked, and
the windows &nd the transom over the
door were shut. The door was broken In
and the dead body of the unfortunate
woman was seen lying on the bed. The
half-empty bottle of carbolic acid lying
on the bureau at the head of the bed told
the rest of the story."
Among her effects was found the fol
lowing letter addressed to Mrs. Kaelber
at Martinez:
RAN FRANCISCO, Dec. 80. IS9S.
Dear Friend Mrs. Kaelber: I will drop you
a few lines to let you know that 1 saw Mrs.
Cunningham on Sixth street last night, and
she told me to write to you and tell you that
the â !â ;. Is very sick Just now, and she wishes
you would come down to the city as soon as
you can. Yours respectfully. nKMtV.
The body was removed to the Morgue
: and an inquest will be held this morning.

Ended Her Misery.
Miss Jennie Carter, vi years of age, who
lived with her brother at 1036 Filbert
street, died yesterday morning- from the
effects of a dose of rat poison taken last
Sunday with suicidal intent. For some
time past Miss Carter has been suffering
with an abcess in the head. Last Sunday
she was taken violently ill. and on being
tpn stioned told her relatives that she had
taken poison to end her misery. A physi
cian was called In who did all in his
power to relieve her sufferings, but
was too late, and after three days of in
tense agony she died at 11 o'clock yester
day. Coroner Hill was notified and gave
a permit for burial.

EXHUMED THE BODY
OF MRS. SLINKEY
THOROUGH CHEMICAL ANALY
SIS WILL BE MADE.
The General Opinion Is That Mrs.
Slinkey Ended Her Own Life
by Hanging.
Quite an army of medical talent gath
ered nt Laurel Hill Cemetery yesterday
afternoon for the disinterment of the
body "f Mrs. James E. Silnkey, found last
Friday hanging in a closet at her home,
110 Ellis street.
Coroner Edmund E. Hill, acting on the
anonymous letter whirh he received last
Sunday, and In which the writer, who
Bigna him or herself. "A Friend," claims
that Mrs. Slinkey met her death by foul
means, headed the corps of physicians
who were present and directed the oper
ations. Besides Dr. Hill there were. Dr.
Zabala, autopsy physician; Dr. E. S.
Howard, professor of anatomy at the Col
lege of Physicians; ex-Coroner Hughes,
Dr. Torney; James E. Slinkey. the hus
band of the deceased; T. J. Crowley, his
attorney, and Detective Dillon, who is
working on the case.
The body Â»as exhumed and removed to
the receiving vault, where the physicians
collected for the dissection. Mr. Slinkey
ed upon the remains of his wife just
before the autopsy and seemed much af
fected. The heart, stomach and bladder
were removed from the body: pieces of
one lung, the liver, spleen and intestines
were afeo taken. A chemical analysis
will be made by L. S. Crackbon at once
for the purpose of discovering any
traces uf poisoning, should they exist. In
this he will ho assisted by Dr. Hill, who
is much interested in the "outcome of the
examination.
All the physicians who were present at
the autopsy yesterday are unanimous in
the opinion that Mrs. Slinkey met her
death from strangulation. All the exter
nal indications, they claim, point to tht-;
mode of life's exit. According to their
examination yesterday afternoon, there
are no external indications of poisoning.
Until a chemical analysis of the detached
organs is made nothing positive c:m be
determined as to the internal indications
of poisoning. The result of this analysis
will not lie known until Friday.
The husband of the dead woman called
on the Coroner again this morning an. 1 ,
he courts the fullest investigation of the
death of his wife. He said she had been
ring from melancholia and mental
depression for some time prior to her
death. A few days before her body was
found slii .<sk< d her physician if death
from strangulation was attended with
greai physical sufferine.
The Coroner and assisting physicians
are prune to believe that the anonymous
writer has played them a joke, yet they
will Investigate the matter fully.
Fortunes Made During th^ War.
A great number of people have made :
fortunes during the war by investing
in securities. In making these invest
ments it required large capital. But
fortunes were also obtained by the fol
lowing parties in return for investments
of sums of money so small they were j
never miÂ«sed. F. Vette. grocer, north
west corner Jones and O'Farrell streets,
San Francisco. Cal., purchased a $1
ticket in the lottery of the Beneficen
oia Publics Company of the City of
Mexico, for which he received $15,000.
Another fortunate -narty was Mr. S.
Cahn. 12?> Montgomery street, San !
Francisco, Cal.. who invested only 50 j
cents and received $1250. The above
amounts were promptly paid at the of
flee of Metzger & Franklin, 35 New
Montgomery street, San Francisco, Cal.
The Lottery of the Beneficencia Pub
lica Company of the City of Mexico is
popular on this coast for the reason j
that so many l.rge prizes have been I
won here. The public is guaranteed j
an hor.est drawing and prompt pay
ment of prize.;, as all drawings are held
in public under the immediate super
vision of the Government, which ap
points Senator Apolinr Castillo Inter
venor, and Antonio Perez for the Treas
ury Department. The old reliable Lot
tery of the H^nefireneia Publiea Com
pany of the City of Mexico, of which
V. Bassetti is manager, is the only lot
tery guaranteed and protected by the
Mexican National Government.

a very easy winner over Sir Urian. A
long price was laid against Casdale. and
his showing fully justified the odds.
Sombre, the jaunty black filly, proved
no match for Rubicon in the final five
furlong dash. The latter was an even
money choice, and won eased up. Som
bre led Novia over the. line half a
length.

Chinese Living at the Mail Dock.
By request of Special Agents Smith and
! Unck, Collector Jackson has prerarorl a
Btatement as to the number of Chinese
i In the Hotel Mongolia on the Mall Dock.
1 They are 221 in number, and some of them
! have been there slnnp May 16, ISPS. The
Collector says he is not responsible for
their detention. They have been refused
. a landing by him, and they are being
kept there by the steamship companies.
, The Collector adds that out or 3.523 Chinese
i arriving at this port in the year IRDB he
! has refused landing to 495. Of the whole
i number of arrivals 757 claimed to be na
j tive born Americans, and the Collector
. refused a landing to 349 of theseâ nearly
half.

LONDON MARKETS.
NEW YORK, Jan. IS.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
Business on the markets here was reduced to
day, but the tone was hard on the confident
expectation that the bank rate will be reduced
to-morrow to 3 per cent. Americans were dull
early but steady on realizing except in Central
Pacifies, which were again bought on rumors
that the reorganization plan is ready. New
York came strong, causing a sharp rally, and
the close was at the best, especially in Atchi
son and Wabash Issues. The latter" s rise sug
gests here the comment that the boom lias
reached the end of the alphabet. London en
thusiasm Is much diminished.
Coppers were buoyant. Tlntos were 34%. the
record. Anacondas hang st 6"sC on stop limits.
Money is unlendable. There is a slight de
mand for cold from the United States at 77s
9%d bid. The week's inilux was Â£149,000.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
'
CHICAGO, Jan. IS.â Several commission peo
ple who had selling orders of a pressing char
acter executed them on the curb before the
opening, and that, together with lower cables
from Liverpool and the Continent, gave wheat
a weak send-off. May opened M<B%c lower at
70@70%c, but there was a great deal of wheat
wanted at the decline and the market ' be
came firm with a slightly upward tendency
during the first hour. Receipts here and In the
Northwest were sufficiently moderate to favor
the bulls rather than the bears, and early In
dications were for a heavy total of seaboard
clearances of wheat and flour. Yesterday's
revelation In the case of Russian shipments
was still In the minds of the traders, however,
which prevented any enthusiastic buying, and
the market ruled rather dull, with May hang
ing around 70-% c.
Bradstreet's statement of the world's visi
ble supply made it 1,562,000 bushels more than it
was a week ago, compared with a reduction of
1 1,000 bushels the same week of last year.
After temporary decline to 70% c, to where May.'
had slowly climbed, the short-lived weakness
being Induced by Bradstreet's statement, tpe
market became comparatively strong. May
reaching 70% c about twenty miutes from ithe
close. At that price a demand sprang up from
traders who had calls sold, and May rose to
70"sÂ®71c, and closed at 70% c. i
Corn was easy and a shade lower to start
v.-Ith, but the demand was good at the decline
and the firmness that developed in wheat
helped corn to maintain Itself against absence
of shipping demand. May closed %@'ic higher.
Selling by a prominent elevator concern
weakened oats early. After this lot was dis
posed of the offerings became light and after
the other markets became stronger the demand
Improved, resulting in a sharp advance. May
closed %@'.;c higher. -
Higher prices for hogs and heavy shipments
of meats started provisions strong. There was
a lot of selling early, however, and as the de
mand was scattered, prices eased off. As soon
as the grain market began to show evidence
of strength this was all changed and buying
became animated, resulting in an advance all
around. May pork closed 7%c higher. The
range In lard and ribs was narrow â¢

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, Jan. 18.â American "Wool and
Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow:
While the wool markets have been quieter
during the past week than for a long time,
they have not been without some interesting
features. One of the most important of these
is the pronounced inquiry for wools grading
one-quarter, three-eighths and one-half blood,
and this inquiry has extended to both domestic
and foreign stocks of this description, Aus
tralian crossbreds in particular having been
well sought for and firm in price. In fact,
these wools are quoted fully a cent per pound
higher than they were o manth ago. Cross
breds had been depressed disproportionately in
comparison with merinos.
In pulled wools there is a firmer feeling,
especially on "B's" and combings, and the
same may be said about carpet wools, which
the buyers have shown more disposition to pur
chase.
THe sales Of thÂ» week in the Boston market
amounted to 2,247,500 pounds domestic and
435.000 pounds foreign, making a total of 2,686, -
MX), against a total of 3,210,500 for the previous
week and a total of 377,000 for the correspond
ing week last year. Since January 1, 1599, sales
amounted to 9,692,000 pounds, against 14,335,000
pounds last year at this time.

THE STOCK MARKET.
The usual trading was done in local securi
ties, with better prices for several sugar and
water stocks and a decline In Gas and Electric.
Mining stocks were weak and quiet.
The San Francisco Gas and Electric Com
pany has declared a regular monthly divi
dend of 60 cents per share, payable February 1.
The morning Star Mining Company of lowa
Hill, riacer County, has declared a monthly
dividend of $4 per share, payable Immediately.
Lieutenant Governor Jacob H. Neff is superin
tendent of this flilne.
The Northern Spy Mining Company of Tintlo
District, Utah, has levied an assessment of
10 cents per share.
The Oceanic Steamship Company has de
clared a dividend of 60c per share, payable
February 1.
The annual meeting of the Fireman's Fund
Insurance Company was held on the 17th.
The election of directors resulted as follows:
D. J. Staples, John O. Earl, John Barton, John
H. Gardiner. John T. Wright, Thomas S.
Chard, W. H. Brown, F. W. Lougee. J. C.
Coleman, John Bermlngham and William J.
Dutton. The old officers were re-elected:
President, D. J. Staples; vice president, Wil
liam J. Dutton; secretary, Bernard Faymon
ville. The gross assets are $3,902,970; rein
surance, $1,295,755: net surplus, $1,361,730.
During the year four quarterly dividends of
$3 each were paid, the last one yesterday.
The Centennial Eureka Mining Company of
Utah paid a monthly dividend of 60 cents per
share on the 16th. The amount was $15,000,
making a total of $2,004,000 since organiza
tion.
The Diamond Consolidated â¢ Mining Com
pany of Utah has levied an assessment or 4
cents per share, delinquent February 20. .
The Andes assessment of 5 cents per share
will be delinquent In the boards to-day.

elected the old directors, with Louis Sloss Jr.,
president, W. Hanson secretary and A. Clarks
general manager.

The final volume of the British Muse
um catalogue of birds has just been com
pleted. This catalogue was begun a quar
ter of a century ago, and is said to be a.
complete list of every bird known. There
are twenty-seven large volumes, all of
which are illustrated by innumerable
woodcuts.

the early morning: tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the

second time column elves the second tide of

And the last or right hand column gives ths

and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of

[ Telephone Main 1508.HOW THE LATE
STORM DEALT
WITH THE SHIPS
Rush for Coal From
Belated Colliers.
SAUSALITO YACHTS SUFFERED
STRIKE OF LONGSHOREMEN ON
THE WRECKED NOYO.
Arrival of the British Ship Wallace
' town â She Was Compelled to
Put Into Coqulmbo in
Distress.
The storm Is over, but tales from ves
.sels that suffered In it will continue to
come In for some time. All the
-..'vessels in port took advantage of the sun
shins yesterday and had their sails out
to dry- A big fleet of fishing boats went
but for the first time in weeks and they
arid the sailors at work on the ships at
anchor gave an appearance of life to
:th.e bay that it has not had for two
.wi-eeks.
. Tho arrival of the overdue colliers made
: ;.thlng3 lively on the front and long strings
;t>f wagons were waiting at each of the
bunkers for a chance to load. The Wel
lington got in from Departure Bay late
Tuesday afternoon, and some of the coal
wagons were in line all night waiting to
get the first load in the morning. The
Charles Nelson came in from Seattle.
Her delay was dua to the fact that her
captain did not like the looks of the
weather, so he lay for a day and a night
in Jseah Bay. The Mlneola from Seattle
and the Wyefield from Nanlamo, B. C,
were seven days coming down, and they
goj tho full benefit of the storm. They
were hove to for thirty-six hours and had
a very rough time of it.
Over at Sausallto the yachtsmen and
others are repairing the damage done by
the southeaster. The San Francisco Yacht
Club's float got loose and drifted down
on Dr. McKenzie's gasoline launch Al
berta. The latter was driven on the
beach and had her bottom considerably
damaged. Later she drifted down on the
â yacht Ripple, and the Alberta had all
. her windows smashed while the Ripple
was badly chafed. The yacht Catherine
had her rudder broken, and a pleasure
boat owned by Mr. Susaville was stove
in by the derelict float. Six feet of the
embankment around the Pacific Yacht
Club's boathouse was carried away, and
the waves made a clean breach through
the building. All the earthworks around
the house were carried away and the
are now stands alone in its glory
in the middle of a lake.
The wrecked steamer Noyo has been
ilncked at Little Main street and the work
nloafllng her is being rushed. Tues
day night J. H. Bullock of Kimball &
got a lot of longshoremen together

TAUGHT HIM PORCH-CLIMBING
grand opera prima donna, reposes in
his prison cell.
In spite of the wondrous taJent Si
monsen, alias James Stewart, the
porch-climbing burglar, has displayed
for his nefarious calling, the man is
first and foremost an actor, and his
gift of the dramatic makes even his
startling thieving propensities pale
into the insignificant. Prison life Is
distasteful to the tenor burglar only
because a jail menu is harmful to the
proper production of clear, sweet notes
and the damp, cold cell is only disa
greeable in the direct ratio that it is
harmful to sensitive vocal chords.
The singing porch-climber fits easily
into the groove of prison life. As yet
he has given no sample of his allur
ing "ahâ ahing" powers, cither to
jailer or inmates, although with tho
same emphasis that he declares his
innocence he protests against the slan
der that he has lost his voice.
"My voice is now stronger and bet
ter than ever," he repeats again and
again, as he dramatically tells his
very fishy theatrical tale that he sup
Drank Carbolic Acid.
Mrs. Gertrude Johnson, who conducts
a shooting gallery at 418 Dupont street,
drank a Quantity of carbolic acid last
night with .suicidal intent, and her death
will probably be the result. About 11
o'clock the woman went out and procured
tin* acid. Returning to the house she
poured the contents of the bottle into a
glasp, but had gulped down but a portion
of it when the glass was wrenched from
her hand by her daughter, Loretta. aged
14 years. She was immediately taken to
the Receiving Hospital, but her condi
tion is very serious and Dr. Morrison,
under whoso treatment she h;uÂ« bron
placed, has given up hope of saving her
llff*. Despondency is said to have been
the cause of her act.
