htm.
It is a tale of noble heroism, with a
dash of hunger and want: a trusting,
unsophisticated hero and an unknown
heroine, for whom the tenor would
rather take a twenty years' sentence
than to reveal her name.
"The Cafe Royal was my undoing,"
explained Simonsen yesterday. "It
is infested by a band of thieves. I was
a stranger in the city. I went up there
and naturally fell In with the people
who frequent the place. I met some
fellows there to whom I advanced
small sums of money, and the articles
they gave me for security it has since
transpired were stolen.
"The jewels the detectives found in
my trunk were given me, by a woman
to pawn for her. She has since left
town and I was awaiting her present
address before carrying out her in
structions. The detectives â those
hounds of the so-called lawâ have
urged me to tell the woman's name.
Never: Rather than have her submit
to the outrages I have been obliged to
submit myself to I would serve, twenty
years. Any way, I am not the kind
or man that tells a woman's nafne."
Simonsen tells his story prettily. He
draws his slight nervous figure 'to its
full ht-ight at the inspiring sacrificing
part, and his baby-blue trusting eyes
fill with tears when he refers to his
misfortune in falling in with the ques
tionable company of the Cafe Royal.
Stoutly the tenor maintains that "the
porch climbing of the Heyman resi
dence that resulted in his arrest is his
very first offense.

want. 1 had an engagement to go to

MIDWEEK NOTES FROM
THE THEATRICAL WORLD
Comic opera leads the various attrac
tions at the theaters. Frank Daniels and
his excellent company are crowding the
Columbia at every performance of "The
Idols Eye." At the Tivoli a very suc
cessful production of "The Wedding Day"
is under way and bids fair for a long
U .. n - , N< 7 a -week Daniels will do "The
\\lzard of the Nile," another success by
the same men that wrote "The Idol's
isye.
At the Alcazar "A Temperance Town"
is enjoying prosperity.
"Yon Yonson" is drawing well at the
California.
"Two Married Men" is the farce comedy
that is pleasing the patrons of the Com
edy.
The Orpheum is offering the usual big
bill this week.
Gerome Helmont, the boy violinist,
plays again in the Sherman & Clay Hall
on Saturday afternoon.
An amateur ballet will be a feature at
the Chutes to-night.

GROCERS AS PURCHASERS.
The Association of Retailers Takes
Action That Will Affect
Local Conditions.
Grocery circles will be profoundly stir
red by the action of the Retail Grocers'
Association last night.
The new hoard of directors, after elect
ing- officers for the ensuing year, took a
stand that will tend to put the smallest
retailer in the city practically on a foot
ing with the largest firms. This was the
appointment of a 1 purchasing committee
consisting of six men, whose combined
purchases, together with the ability to
buy of the other stockholders of the or
ganization, will enable the Retail Grocers'
Association to contract for goods in
larger quantities than most of the other
wholesale houses.
Joseph A. Stulz and Supervisor John
Laekmann were elected president and
vice president respectively. The pur
chasing committee is to consist of Alex Ir
vine (5 stores), W. F. Harris (3 stores),
Andrew J. Knox (] stores), President Jo
seph A. Stulz (2 stores). E. I. Flioger (1
store) and Secretary John C. Nohmann.
Twelve new members were reported for
the past two weeks.
The pure food law was condemned. The
directors expressed their desire to see the
lawn enforced, but thought the State
should devote itself to manufacturers and
manufacturers' agents and leave retail
ers to the local authorities.-
A committee was appointed to act with
a similar committee from the Butchers'
Board of Trade to advance the amend
ments proposed to the debtor law.

IN a drese Phlrt, rumpled and rather H

while Tivoii favorite and brother to A
Madame Saville, the world renowned <
ment for several days. If a singer
Is not properly fed he is in no condi
tion to sing. I could not have my first
appearance in Seattle a disastrous
failure, so I made for that window,
intending with the booty to purchase
proper food."
Simonsen is very much aggrieved at
the officers of the law, who he says
are trying to get him to confess to
crime he never committed. "This is
my first and only offense," he says,
and with his dubious tale of the Cafe
Royal he tries to explain the stolen
articles and the pawn tickets for other
pilfered trifles found in his trunk.
Simonsen has sung with success in
Southern California, and also at the
Tivoii, where he recently appeared as
Renee, the tenor role in "Luzette."
The management at the Tivoii was
we m ll pleased with the porch-climbing
tenor. His voice was pleasant, his
appearance agreeable and his acting
light and easy. He only severed his
engagement at that houÃe because, the
holiday production of the "Yellow
Dwarf " found no part for him.
Madame Saville, the sister of 'he in
carcerated tenor, is one of the world's
famous song birds. As an artist she
ranks with Melba and has for many
years been a favorite in all the great
musical centers of the Old Worlrt.
Simonsen claims that be has ap
peared with success in all the stand
ard grand and comic operas, and con
fesses to a fondness for the role of
Romeo. Probably his agility in scal
ing Juliet's balcony gave him his first
hint in the direction of porch climb
ing.
Simonsen was yesterday held to an
swer before the Superior Court by
Judge Conlan In 12000 bonds on the
charge of burglary, for entering Hey
man s house Saturday night. The four
other charges against him were con
tinued until to-morrow.

if she made any attempt to have him ar
rested he would kill her.
Death of Professor Schmitz.
Professor Joseph L. Sehmitz. the well
known musician, expired in this city yes
terday. Of the many musicians who came
to this coast in the days of '49 he was,
perhaps, the best known and one of the
highest esteemed. He wielded the baton
in many opera houses that are of the
past and guided the wavering notes of
almost every celebrity on the stage.
Among the old houses in which he had or
chestras were the Metropolitan Theater
on Montgomery, near Jackson, the Ma
guire Opera-house on Washington street
and the American Theater on Sansome
street.
.v. i ""I 11361 " of successful artists made
their debut before the aristocracy of the
early sixties under his direction the list
of their names Including Madame Parepa
Rosa, Jennie Lind. Anna Bishop. lima
dl MurÃka, Madame Biscaciante and the
islanchl opera troupe. Deceased was 77
years of age and leaves a widow, live sons
and a daughter. One of his sons E E
Bchmltz. is ;t well-known theater loader.
Marie Barna, a California girl,
tells how she was selected to sing
leading roles at the great Wagner
festival, in next Sunday's Call.
The Tobacco Order Will Stand.
Internal Reveune Collector Lynch will
enforce the order of the Commissioner of
Internal Revenue with regard to the pro
hibition of tho sale of leaf tobacco ex
cept in the original packages. The re
quest of dealers In this State for a sus
pension of the order pending the forward
ing of a protest to Commissioner Wilson
has been denied and the small manu
facturers of cigars must make the best
of it.

SUGGESTED
REDUCTIONS IN
HEALTH OFFICE
Secretary Dwyer as a
Reformer.
WOULD CUT DOWN EXPENSES .
HEALTH OFFICER A. P. O'BRIEN
WELCOMED BACK.
Dr. John Gallwey Publicly Thanked
for Services He Has Rendered
the City During the Past
Eight Months. |
. . j
â  â¢ â  - â > '"'
At the meeting of the Board of
Health last night Secretary J. J. I
Dwyer presented an extended report
showing how the expenses of the de
partment could be reduced $700 per
month without impairing the service.
There will be a deficit of $3000 in the
funds of the board at the end of its
term unless rigid economy is prac
ticed. He suggested that the board re
duce traveling and other expenses of
the various employes from $312 50 to
$162 50 per month, thus saving $150 in
that period. He thought the positions
of cigar factory inspector, laundry in
spector, bakery inspector and one gar
bage inspector should be abolished.
Their duties should be performed by
other officials of the department. Their
dismissal would mean a saving of $225.
He also suggested cutting The salary
of the veterinary surgeon from $150 to
$75. He would do away with the as
sistant vaccinator, saving another $50
per month. Finally he would reduce the
ten food inspectors to a force of six.
The total saving to the Health Depart
ment would be $700. Should these sug
gestions be adopted there would be no
deficiency. The matter was referred to
the Finance Committee.
Dr. Bazet suggested the location in
some convenient central place, near
the Receiving Hospital, of a munici
pal disinfecting station. It should
be open to the public free of charge.
It would be used to stop the spread of
infectious disease by sterilizing the
clothes, bedding, carpets and all
articles connected with infected pa
tients. Dr. Bazet also reported at
length upon the necessity of a steriliz
ing plant at the City and County Hos
pital.
The board recommended that the at
tention of the Supervisors be called to
the nuisance on the : Fair property
over at Black Point. It was sug
gested that they extend the sewers to
tide water.
Chemist Green of the board reported
that of fourteen samples of coffee ex
amined by him only two were pure.
The others were adulterated with chic
ory, flgseeds and cornhusks.
Attorney Burdette, representing Claus
Spreckels, appeared before the board
to protest against a nuisance and a
menace to health. This Is a meat pack
ing establishment that has been de
clared a nuisance by all who have been
brought in contact with it. Dr. Gallwey i
condemned it, saying it was a menace
to the health of persons living near by
and should be removed. Upon motion
of Dr. Fitzgibbon the proprietor was i
given one week in which to abate the
nuisance.
The meeting was not without its hu
mors. Dr. Hereford reported that the
appearance at the Pesthouse of a man
with variolold resulted in the employes
getting up and quitting. The disaf
fected employes were the cook and i
night watchman. The patient has not
suffered, as a special nurse was em
ployed, and Dr. Tlllman volunteered
also to take charge of the patient. For
this the board thanked him.
"*â  Dr. W. P. Agnew applied for the posi
tion as representative of the board at
the forthcoming six-day bicycle race.
This was referred to the committee on
Receiving Hospital, Dr. Williamson re
marking that was the Hace the men
would be most likely reach ultimately.
Matthew Kerr asked that a bottle of
medicine he purchased from a Market
street druggist be analyzed. It was for
distemper in dogs, and those doctored
with it died. The request was granted.
F. J. Corbett resigned his position of
inspector of garbage transportation.
The position of Leopold Popper, nipht
watchman at the Pesthouse, was de
clared vacant upon motion of Dr. Bazet.
C. A. Stafford, assistant chemist at
the City and County Hospital, resigned.
Superintendent Sussdorf, of the hos
pital, reported that he had to use the j
vestry and chapel in order. to isolate a
case of scarlet fever. His action was
approved.
Dr. John Gallwey. who has been act-
Ing Health Officer in the absence of Dr.
A. P. O'Brien, tendered his formal
resignation. Upon his retiring Mayor
Phelan thanked him for the splendid
services he had rendered the city dur
ing the past eight months.
Dr. O'Brien was escorted to his place
and welcomed by the Mayor, who said
that all were proud of him for the cood
he had done at the front. Dr. O'Brien
thanked the board for the reception ac
corded him. He al?o thanked Dr. Gall
wey, who, he said, had given a vast
amount of attention to the office and
had made a great sacrifice in aiding
him. He told of the boys of the gal
lant First Regiment at Manila, of their
general health and of their homesick
ness.

WAS MUNCE'S
MISSION WORK
DEEP DESIGN ?
Preacher Is Seriously
Charged.
ACCUSED OF GRAVE FRAUD
REVELATIONS EXPECTED IN
THE SANDERS WILL CASE.
Heir-at-Law Claims That William
Munce Used Undue Influence to
Gain Possession of Mrs.
Sanders' Estate.
Sensational dove-l'ipments are prom
ised in an action filed In the- Superior
Court yesterday by Attorney Charles L.
Patton, whereby Mrs. Martha Peterson
puos for the revocation of th 2 last will
and testament of the 'ale- Sarah J.
Sanders, who was familiarly known
(luring her lifetime as "the seamen's
friend." The testator died January 3,
1898, and on the 20th of the same month
her alleged will was admitted to pro
bate. I'nder the terms of the will,
which boars the dato of November 7.
1896, the chief beneficiary is William
WILLIAM MUNCE, the Ex- Mission -Worker,
Against Whom Serious Charges Are Made.
â¢ ; Munce, a mission-worker, who had for I
â  many years made his home in the San- 1
Â ders household. Mrs. Peterson, a niece
and solt- surviving relative of the testa- â 
; .t6r, was cut off with a paltry $100, while
-Munce was handsomely remembered in ,
'â¢'â '. a bequest of $4000; the remainder of the ;
'.. estate was left to William Munce and i
â Alb- rt Raymond, in trust, to be applied !
"to the support, maintenance and as- I
. '.'sistance of such seafaring men and Bea-
Btjen as may, in their Judgment, need : ,
â¢ -and deserve the same." 'â 
Mrs. Peterson charges that at the J
time th- will was executed Mrs. Sand- j i
*rs was in a weakened mental and pay- ;
sical condition; that William Munce | :
"wormed himself Into her confidence" j
i>y "falsely pretending to be a minister
of the gospel, and that he secured the ! |
execution of the will in his favor by â 
gross fraud." A very suspicious cir
cumstance in the premises is that the
â  insurance policy on the property of the
estate known as the . eamen's Home. ]
on liealÂ«- street, bears the â¢'X-inark" of .
Mrs. Sanders, and accompanying this
mark is the attest of a notary public
to the fact that "Mrs. Sarah J. Sanders
is ur.abli- i" read or write," while tho i
alleged will now in controversy con
tains the signature of the same Mrs,
Sanders in very legible handwriting:.
It Is fifteen years Bince AVUliam
' Munce arrived in this city from Chi
cago. According to his own story he
had been a captain on the great lakes,
but his vessel had gone down in a
Storm, and, harassed by ill-fortune, he
had finally come to this coast to try
â¢ and better his condition. Being in
hard straits he sought the Mariners' j
Church, and not only received aid, but
was soon employed to do mission work ;
among bay sailors.
Rev. J. Rowell, pastor of the Mari
ners' Church, corner of Sacramento and
Drumm streets, remembers well Mr.
Munce's advent. "Munce enlisted in
mission work under Rev. W. D.
Bishop," said the venerable minister
' last evening, "and was supported by i
subscriptions from church members. !
One of the most zealous members of !
our congregation at that time was Mrs.
Blisa J. Sanders, and, as the subscrip- j
tions for Munce's support were not very i
large the good old lady took him to '
board at her Seamen's Home on Beale I
street. Then trouble arose over the J
purchase of a boat for missionary work
and Munce's connection with the
church was severed. Rev. Mr. Bishop
warned Mrs. Sanders against Munce, j
but his warnings proved of no avail.
The old lady was not sound, either in <]
mind or body, for a number of years j
prior to her demise. Whenever I called
to see her Mr. Munce was invariably
present during the interview. He had
strong influence over Mrs. Sanders.
The old lady told me repeatedl* that
she had made provision for the\con
tinuance of gospel work among seamen
at the old home on Beale street."
When John Sanders died in 1882 he
left his widow, Sarah J., about $45,000
worth of property. To-day all that is
left to show for it is the Beale-street j
house, valued at from $7000 to $10,000. |
Mrs. Sanders was an economical lady,
nevertheless, although she had a kindly,
charitable heart. Munce managed the
old lady's property for thirteen years.
His room in the house was next to that
of Mrs. Sanders, with an ear-tube be
tween. Munce was paid, Mrs. Peterson
states, $5 for every meeting he held In
the house for the conversion of sailors.
He frequented the Peniel ?nd Salvation
Army missions and was well known
among them while Mrs. Sanders lived.
Since her death Munce has ceased ail
mission work. He no longer seeks the
conversion of sailors.
The Seamen's Home, Beale street,
well insured, took flre one midnight ol
last June, and only the very prompt
work of the Fire Department prevented
the complete destruction of the prop
erty. The circumstances pointed strong
ly toward incendiarism. Since that
time Peter Peterson, the husband of
the petitioner, has slept nightly in the
building. .
Last December all the furniture of
the Seamen's Home, saving the con
tents of one room, was sold at auction
by Munce, as trustee, and the house
th.it Mrs. Sanders hoped would be a
home for needy seamen is now a home
for no-body but Munce.
GRACE SYNDICATE
GIVEN A HEARING
Congressman McClure's Speech Re
veals the Names of Men Back
ing the Venture.
NEW YORK, Jan. IS.â A Washing
ton special to the Herald says: It was
not realized until the hearing was given
to-day by the House Committee o-n
Commerce .to the Grace-Eyre-Cragin
Nicaragua canal syndicate just how
strong is the combination behind W.
R. Grace in the scheme to build the
Nicaragua canal. Every one has
known about the Grace syndicate, but
the names of the men who really com
pose it had been carefully concealed
until they were revealed to-day by Mr.
McClure in a speech before the Com
mittee on Commerce. Then it became
known that the men who propose build
ing the canal are among the best
known capitalists in New York.
Besides Mr. Grace they include D. O.
Mills. Levi P. Mortem, John Jacob As
tor, George D. Havens, John A. McCall,
Warner Miller, H. B. Hollins, W. H.
Gelshenen (president of the Garfield
National Bank), E. R. Ladow, George
C. Williams of the Chemical National
Bank, James Tillman. H. H. Rogers of
the Standard Oil Company, Robert Goe
let, John D. Crimmins. W. D. Sloane,
James T. Woodward, George Westing
house and E. J. Berwind.
LONDON, Jan. 19.â The Standard,
commenting editorially this morning
upon thp debate regarding the Nica
ragua canal in the United States Con
gress, protests strongly against the
Idea that Great Britain would be will
ing to abrogate the Clayton-Bulwer
treaty unless compensated therefor and
unless the neutralization of the canal
were secured beyond Question.
Edward Everett Hale has a sermon
in next Sunday's Call.
PRESENTMENT THE
CAUSE OF TREASON
General Mercier's Theory as to
Wfyy Captain Dreyfus Is
Guilty.
PARIS, Jan. 18.â The Solr to-day publishes another interview with
General Mercier, who was Minister of War at the time of the condem
nation of Dreyfus. General Mercier reiterates his former statements
and reasserts that the bordereau was certainly tho work of Dreyfus and
not of Esterhazy, who was not aware of Its existence until long sub
sequent to the trial. Under the plea of professional secrecy he de
clined to explain how the War Office obtained the bordereau. The se
cret dossier, he said, consisted of War Ministry documents fully proving
the inferences deduced from the bordereau. The suggestion that it con
tained letters from a foreign sovereign he characterized as "a pure in
vention," as was also, he declared, the statement that M. Casimir-
Perier resigned the Presidency of the republic on account of the Drey
fus affair.
Regarding the motives that prompted Dreyfus to commit the trea
son imputed General Mercier said: "Dreyfus was too fond of the so
ciety of women of a certain class. Had it not been for his reputa
tion in this respect he would have left the military college among the
first three of his class, but owing to his known immoral proclivities he
was placed eleventh. I think ho resented this and that his treason was
probably an act of revenge for It. Dreyfus was also in the habit of-talk
ing unpatriotically. A general was once obliged to censure him for say
ing that the Reichstanders preferred to belong to Germany, as they
were better treated, Dreyfus adding that he quite agreed with them."
Major Esterhazy arrived in Paris to-night. He declined to be in
terviewed.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, JANUARY 19, 1899.
FIGHT AGAINST
MATT S. QUAY
Pennsylvania Legisla
tors Aroused.
WILL DEPOSE GENERAL GOBIN
LIEUTENANT GOVERNOR ACTS
VERY ARBITRARILY.
Declares the Joint Assembly Ad
journed When Three-Fourths of
the Members Voted
Otherwise.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
HARRISBURG, Pa., Jan. 18.â The
feeling between the candidates, their
managers and their followers in the
great battle for United States Senator
has been intensified by the rulings of
Lieutenant Governor Gobin at to-day's
joint assembly of the Senate and House.
Gobin refused to entertain an appeal
from his decision excluding a series of
rules regulating the procedure of the
conference and declaring the Assembly
adjourned when three-fourths of the
Senators and members voted otherwise.
The anti-Quay Republicans and Dem
ocrats were hurriedly called together
after the session adjourned by Senator
William Flynn of Allegheny (R.), chair
man of the anti-Quay organization in
the Assembly. The roll call showed 145
Senators and Representatives present.
Of these five Republicans who voted
for QuajÂ» participated in the meeting
and indicated their sympathy with the
movement. A committee of seven was
appointed to prepare a series of reso-
lutions to be submitted to an adjourned
meeting which was held this evening
in the hall of the House of Represen
tatives.
The hall of the House was crowded
with legislators and politicians when
the night meeting was called to order
by Chairman Bliss. Mr. Flynn pre
sented the report. It recites the ex
cluded rules and the right to prescribe
such, declaring that the presiding offi
cer's action violated all rules and pre
cedents, and resolves "that, as the joint
Assembly has the undoubted right to
govern its own procedure and to elect
or change a presiding officer at pleas
ure, the same rules be offered at to
morrow's meeting of the Joint Assem
bly and their consideration demanded
prior to any ballot for United States
Senate." This report is signed by nine
teen Senators and 120 Representatives,
four more than attended the day meet
ing.
In explaining the resolution Mr.
Flynn said there was no law or con
stitutional mandate that allows the
Lieutenant Governor to preside at a
joint Assembly of the Senate and House
to elect a United States Senator; it was
simply a matter of courtesy.
The report was adopted and copies
were distributed among the Senators
and members for signatures.
A motion was offered by Senator
David Martin of Philadelphia (R.),
which was adopted, that a committee
of seven be continued with power to
call the independent Republicans and
Democrats together any time the neces
sity arises. Mr. Fox read section 6,
article IV, of the constitution, to show
that Mr. Gobin, who is a brigadier
general in the volunteer army, has no
right to preside at the conference. The
section follows: "No member of Con
gress or person holding any office un
der the United States or this State
shall exercise the office of Governor or
j Lieutenant Governor."
RETIRES SILVER TO
THE BACKGROUND
Newlands Making His Fight for the
Senate on the Railroad
Issue Alone.
CARSON, New, Jan. 18.â The town is
still talking over the speech delivered
by Congressman Newlands last night.
He denounced the methods of the rail
road and retired the silver issue to the
background. Stewart has made no
reply to Newlands' speech, but places
his hope in his silver record.
The contest in the Assembly
this morning between Shier and Dooley
resulted in Shier withdrawing his peti
tion, leaving the ground clear for
Dooley. Dooley is a Democrat and
will lend his support to a Democratic
nominee.
Some of the most conservative mem
bers believe the Senatorial fight will
result in a deadlock. R. M. Clarke and
Hock Mason are after the Democratic
vote, which draws strength from both
Newlands and Stewart.
Petitions were presented this morn
ing in the House and Senate from
Douglas and Esmeralda counties pro
testing against Stewart's return to the
Senate. They are both based on his
railroad record. They were referred to
committees from the respective coun
ties. They will not be reported until
Monday and the effect will be lost.
ROMEO WINNER
OF THE DERBY
Valiente's Son Captures
the Trophy.
BEATS HIGH - CLASS DOGS
RESULTS OF FIELD TRIALS AT
BAKERSFIELD.
Despite the Sparseness of the Cover,
Some Fine Work Is Witnessed
Daily by the Sports
men.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BAKERSFIELD, Jan. 18.â The six
teenth annual trials of the Pacific
Coast Field Trial Club continued to
day. In the derby fifteen high
class dogs started. Considering
the scare cover their work
was remarkable. Romeo, a setter dog,
owned by John Haauerwas, won first
money, a handsome trophy presented
by the Field of Sports. Romeo is out
of Lady Stamboul, by Valiente. Merry
Heart, a setter dog by Merry Monarch
out of Sweetheart's Last, owned by
Albert Betz, was second. Ashbury, a
pointer dog, owned by Otto Fendner,
was third. Valsmark spoiled his chance
for a place by chasing rabbits and get
ting lost.
The all-age stake started after the
finish of the derby. Romeo, the win
ner of the derby, was an additional
starter. The dogs' run follows: J. C.
Lucas' Alex C. vs. N. I. Donovan's Sen
ator P., Henry Gerber's Fannie G. with
F. B. Naylor's Countess Glenbeierh.
Stockdale Kennels' Sams Bow with J.
C. Terry's Orion, Stockdale Kennels'
Cuba of Kenwood with M. H. Hick
man's Count Harold. Romeo a bye.
All these dogs, except Romeo, Orion
and Count Harold, are pointers. All the
dogs showed up splendidly. The gen
eral opinion is that never have as many
high-class dogs started in one stake
The fine ranging and speed of Senator
P., Alex C, Countess Glenbeieh and
Romeo, and the point work of Sams
Bow, Orion and Cuba of Kenwood make
it hard to pick winners.
The second series will be called to
morrow and probably finished. The
Judges named the following does to run
In the second series: Alex C. with Cuba
of Kenwood, Sams Bow with Senator P.

EXPLODES A BOMB
UNDER THE BILL
Allison's Amendment to
Canal Measure.
PROVIDES FOR BOND ISSUE
VOTE ON THE MATTER POST
PONED AT LAST MOMENT.
In the House Opposition Is Shown to
the Proposed Subsidy to the
Pacific Cable Com
pany.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 18.â The feature
of to-day's session of the Senate was a
speech delivered by Bacon (D.) of Geor
gia, in support of his resolution declar
ing that the United States would not
assume sovereignty over the Philippine
Islands. Bacon is one of the orators of
the Senate, and as he gave himself free
rein, not confining himself to notes, he
spoke strongly and effectually. His
peroration. In which he pictured the
horrors of some of England's methods
of controlling her colonial subjects, was
a beautiful and forceful bit of word
painting.
Following Bacon's speech, the Nica
ragua canal bill was taken up. and re
mained under discussion until 6 o'clock.
Amendment after amendment was vot
ed down, and until almost the last min
ute it seemed likely that a vote on the
measure would be reached, but lust as
a final vote was about to be called for
Allison (R.) of lowa exploded a bomb
under the bill by offering an amand
ment providing that the Secretary of
the Treasury should issue bonds to
raise money with which to construct
the canal. The friends of the measure
then consented to an adjournment.
HOUSE OPPOSITION TO
THE PACIFIC CABLE
WASHINGTON, Jan. 18.â The com
mittee on interstate and foreign com
merce had the right of way in the
House to-day, and succeeded in pass
ing quite*a number of bills of minor
importance, most of which authorized
the construction of lighthouses, foe
signals, etc. Then the bill to errant the
Pacific Cable Company a subsidy of
$100,000 a year for twenty years for the
construction of and operation of a cable
was called up and a very spirited de-_
bate, which consumed the remainder of
the day, followed.
The opposition was headed by Corliss
(R.) of Michigan, who advocated the
construction of a Government cab'o.
No conclusion was reached at the hour
of adjournment, and as the special or
der under which the House v.as oper
ating to-day expired with ihe adjourn
ment, and as objection v.as made to
fixing another day for its consideration,
it goes over indefinitely until tiie com
mittee can get another day or until
a special order is adopted.

PROPOSALS.
PROPOSALS for the Erection of a Police Sta-
tion on Seventeenth Street, and for the erec-
tion of Police Patrol Stables on Eighteenth
Street.
Office of the Clerk of the Board of Super-
visors of the City and County of Ban Fran-
cisco, January 17, 1899.
In accordance with Resolution No. 2184
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervisors
SEALED PROPOSALS will be received in
open session Of the Board of Supervisors on
MONDAY AFTERNOON, January 30. 1899, '
from 2 to 2:30 o'clock, for the erection of a I
police station on Seventeenth street and for I
the erection of police patrol stables on Eigh-
teenth street, in accordance with the modified
plans and specifications filed In the office of
the Clerk of the Board of Supervisors on De-
cember 30, IS9S. and the conditions set forth
In the said specifications, prepared by Messrs
Shea & Shea, architects, and to be seen at
the office of said architects at No. 26 Mont-
gomery street, all the work to be performed
to the satisfaction of the Chief of Police.
The work of construction to be done in
strict accordance with the said modified plans
and specifications, and under the supervision
and direction of Messrs. Shea & Shea, archi-
â  tects, and the Chief of Police.
Eight hours to constitute a day's work for
all workmen employed upon the work, and
the minimum rate of wages to be paid on
said work to be two ($2) dollars per day.
Bidders will estimate separately and state a
price:
Firstâ For which they will furnish all ma-
terial and work required in the erection of
' the police station and the police patrol
. stables (except for plumbing and gasflttlng)
Secondâ For which they will furnish all the
plumbing and gasfltting- material and the
work required In the construction of the
same In the police station and the police
patrol stables.
The party to whom the contract may be
awarded will be required, prior to or at the
time of the execution of said contract, to pay
the cost of advertising this and a former
notice In three dally newspapers.
In order to preserve uniformity and to fa-
cilitate the award the Board has resolved to
receive no bid unless made upon blank forms
prepared by the committee, and a certified
check on some bank in the City and County
of San Francisco for the sum of five thousand
($5000) dollars deposited by the bidder with
his bid, and made payable to the Clerk of the
Board of Supervisors, conditioned If the pro-
posal be accepted and the contract awarded;
and if the bidder shall fail or neglect to pay
the ' printing charges, execute the contract
and give the required bond within six days
after the- award is made, then and In that
case the said sum shall be paid into the City
. and County Treasury by said Clerk as liqui-
dated damages for such failure and neglect
' Blanks furnished by the Clerk.
The Board reserves the right to reject all
bids if the public good bo require.
â TNO. A. RUSSELL. Clerk.

CONQUEROR OF
SANTIAGO IS
RETURNED HOME
General Shafter Ar
rived Last Night.
WELCOMED BY THE MAYOR
PUBLIC RECEPTION AT THE PAL
ACE TO-MORROW EVENING.
General Shafter Tells Why He Was
Attacked by the Yellow Press
Reporters â Simply Did
His Duty.
After an absence of nearly nine
months Major General W. R. Shatter
returned to San Francisco at 7 o'clock
last night from Washington. D. C. He
! was accompanied by his aid-de-camp.
Major Hubert 11. Noble, Captain E. H.
Plummer and Captain W. H. McKit
trick and wife. J. N. Shafter of San
Antonio, a brother of the general, with
his wife and son, were also in the
party. In addition to these there was
Mrs. Captain McKlttrick'a maid and
Mariano Uterro, a 16-<year-old Cuban
boy, who returned to this country from
Santiago with General Shafter as his
valet. The distinguished party was
met at the Oakland mole by General
R. H. Warfield, Colonel W. R. Smed
berg. Colonel J. C. Currier, George WÂ«
â â  Spencer.
General Shafter received a warm wel
. come and he returned the same with
equal warmth, at the same time ex
' -pressing himself as being more than
'. glad to be back in San Francisco. The
general wore his full uniform as a
major general, with the exception that
in place of his army cape he wore a light
top coat. Captains Noble and McKit
trick wore in uniform. As soon as the
general and party had shaken hands
with the members of the Loyal Legion,
the entire party went aboard the
Berkeley. General Shafter looked to
be in excellent health and is quite as
â  robust as when he left here last
April. He seems to have en
tirely recovered from the effects of the
malaria which he contracted in the
Cuban campaign. He talked very
pleasantly and freely.
He said: "I am glad to get back in
California. There are many reasons
why I wanted to return. My home is
â  here and besides I have many ties that
bind me to the West, to say nothing of
my business interests here. At pres
ent I aiff in excellent health and I be
lieve that I have entirely recovered
from the malarial fever that I con
tracted in Cuba. Of course, I am aware
that the public desires to know what I
have to say now that I have returned.
There has been much written and more
said about my campaign, but the fact
remains that I was sent to do a certain
thing and I did it. This war was a
very different one from the civil war.
We had not only the Spaniards to fight,
but we had sickness as well. The
climatic conditions were such that sick
ness could not be prevented. The only
wonder to me is that it did not break
out before it did. As for myself I lost
sixty pounds in thirty days, but de
spit-- this fact I was in my saddle every
day with the exception of one.
"As for the attacks that were made
on me by the representatives of the
Journal and Richard H. Davis, they
can be accounted for when the animus
is known. Davis was angered at me
because I would not permit him to be
land' 1 before the army or any of the
other war correspondents. I had my
reasons for not allowing any one to
land before the army did, and I was
forced to tell Davis that no matter
how many papers he represented he
would have to obey my orders. The
trouble with the Journal arose because
when Santiago surrendered I had to ar
rest three of its correspondent and send
them bark to New York. They went
into the city after it surrendered, when
it was filled with Cubans and armed
Spaniards (for there had not been
time for all of the Spaniards to lay
down their arms) and began to put up
flaring poster?;, on which was 'Remem
ber the Maine.' To the Spaniards this
was like a red flag to a bull, and if I
had not had them taken down at once
there would have been trouble.
"Again it has been charged that I
was a friend of the administration.
This is too absurd to be discussed. I
simply did my duty and accomplished
the work that was given me to do. It
is well enough for those who desire to
attack the administration to use me as
their target. Regarding the work of
our men in Cuba too much cannot be
said of them. They deserve all the
praise that can be bestowed on them.
There is no questioning the bravery of
the Spaniards either, but they could
not fight our men. There can be made
no comparison in marksmanship."
There was no suggestion of boast in
General Shafter's remarks. He cour
teously answered all questions asked
him. When asked what were his plans
for assuming command of this depart
ment he stated that he would relieve
General Merriam of command at noon
to-day.
As soon as the Berkeley had made
her slip on this side Mayor Phelan at
the head of a delegation of prominent
citizens came aboard to welcome the
general. As the party came down to
enter their carriages an eager crowd
surged about General Shafter, but the
police were on hand and they pre
vented it from impeding his progress.
General Merriam had thoughtfully pro
vided his carriage, and into this Gen
eral Shafter, Captain McKlttrick and
â¢ wife and Major Noble entered and were
rapidly driven to the Palace. The rest
of the party with Mayor Phelan fol
lowed. The general's arrival at the
Palace created much excitement among
the guests, who were assembled in the
lobby expecting him. As he 'alighted
he was received by Major General Mer
riam. The two veterans exchanged
warm greetings. Captain McKittrick
â  registered for the party and as soon as
. they were assigned rooms they retired
to them to prepare for dinner.
General Shaft <-r will take up his resi
dence at Fort Mason this morning, and
at noon to-day he will formally relieve
General Merriam of command of this
' department. The public reception to be
tendered General Shafter will be held
at the Palace at 9 o'clock to-morrow
evening. The committee of arrange
ments met yesterday and completed the
final preparations. The reception is to
be one of the largest of its kind ever
â â¢ held in this city. In addition to the
committee of arrangements, which con
sists of Mayor Phelan, Colonel A. S.
Hubbard, Colonel J. C. Currier, General
â¢ R. H. Warfield, Colonel C. Mason Kinne,
' Colonel W. R. Smedberg, Colonel E. A.
Denicke, W. M. Bunker, Sol Cahen, T.
C. Masteller and Colonel A. G. Hawes,
there is to be a committee of one hun
dred representative citizens, who are
to form the reception committee proper.
They will meet in the parlors of the
hotel at 8:30 o'clock. There will be a
GENERAL WILLIAM R. SHAFTER, SURROUNDED BY THE FRIENDS WHO WENT UP
THE ROAD TO MEET HIM YESTERDAY.
sufficient number of police on hand to
keep the crowd in line and moving.
The reception committee is composed
of the representatives of the city's so
cial, commercial and army life. The
veterans of the Grand Army of the Re
public, in full uniform, are to be pres
ent as an escort of honor to General
Shafter. Other organizations will also
be present. The Fourth Cavalry Band
will be stationed in the court and will
furnish the music during the reception.
The band was courteously tendered by
General C. E. Compton, commanding
the Presidio. All of the army officers
now on duty here will be present.
One of the most interesting person
ages in the general's party is Mariano
Uterro, his valet. The boy is only 16.
He is very handsome and speaks Eng
lish. He used to shave General Shafter
when he was in Santiago and the gen
eral became so attached to him that he
offered to bring him to this country as
his valet and the boy accepted. He is
much interested in all that he sees, and
General Shafter declares that he has
lost him on an average of every five
minutes since he has had him. On the
trip out he had to keep an eye on the
boy to prevent his getting off at every
station.
In addition to the public reception to
be tendered General Shafter on Friday
evening, the prominent citizens of the
city, desiring to further express to the
general the high esteem in which he is
held by them, have arranged for a
series of entertainments in his honor.
On Saturday evening he will be the
guest of the Union League Club at a
dinner, and on next Wednesday he will
be entertained by the members of the
Pacific Union.

THE SAN FBANCISCO CALL, THUKSDAY, JANUAEY 19, 1899.
BEEF INTERESTS
MAY SECURE MILES'
COURT=MARTIAL
Detail of the Court Selected to
Try Charges Against Com=
missary=General Eagan.
NEW YORK, Jan. 18.â There is serious talk in administration cir
cles as to the advisability of cdurt-martialing General Miles or of reliev
ing him from duty as commanding general of the army. It is manifest
that the President and Secretary of War are not yet done with General
Miles, and that it is proposed to take action of some kind against him;
but what its character will be will depend largely upon developments
of the Eagan trial and of the report of the War Investigation Commis
sion. The administration is incensed at General Miles because of the
tenacity with which he has continued the beef investigation, notwith
standing that the war commission found, as a result of its inquiry, that
the beef furnished to the army was good and had not undergone chemi
cal treatment. Beef packers and members of Congress are stiffening the
backbone of the administration by the use of their influence, and it
would not be surprising to the friends of General Miles if the matter
should culminate in his relief or court-martial.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 18.â The detail
for the court-martial which is to try
Commissary. General Eagan on charges
growing out of his statements before
the War Investigation Commission last
week, in which he - attacked General
Miles, was made public at the War De
partment to-night. The court is made
up of thirteen army, officers, of whom
Major General -Wesley Merritt is at the
head, and a judge advocate, and it is
to meet in this city on Wednesday, the
25th inst, or as soon thereafter as
practicable, this qualification being
necessary because a number of the
members are at distant points and will
require some days to adjust their af
fairs and reach this city. All the of
ficers composing the court, save one,
are from the regular army, a number
of whom, however, during the war, ac
cepted volunteer rank and still hold
those commissions. Major General
Butler is the only one who is not con
nected with ' the regular army. Ad
jutant General Corbin' returned early
In the evening from his trip to New
York, where he went to witness the de
parture of the transport Grant for
Manila 'and came to the War Depart
ment .about 3:30 o'clock. He then
signed the 'formal order promulgating
the detail for the court, which is as
follows:
AVAR DEPARTMENT, ADJUTANT
GENERALS OFFICE, WASHINGTON,
D C . Jan. 18â The following order is pub
lished for the information and guidance
of all 1 concerned: .
WAR DEPARTMENT,
WASHINGTON, Jan. 18, 1899.
By direction of the President a general court
martial is appointed to meet in thin city at 10
o'clock a. m. on Wednesday, the 25th day of
January, 1599, or as soon thereafter as prac
ticable, for the trial of Brigadier General
Charles P. Kagan, Commissary General of Sub
sistence, U. S. A., and such other persons as
may be brought before it.

sary general, will be tantamount to the
relief of that officer from his present
duties and he will turn over its affairs
either to some one designated to act
by the Secretary of War, or to the of
ficer,, next, in charge of the department.

sary department is now on duty with
General" Eagan and it may be that he
will be detailed to take charge of the
office.

of the adjutant general's office to
night is followed the probability is that
General Eagan will not be placed un
der actual arrest and deprived of any of.
his liberties pending and during the
trial, but will be subjected only to con
structive and technical arrest during
that time.

for a few days he will file a few suits
that promise sensational reading for
the mining public.
Nevills is a third owner in the great
Rawhide mine, the remaining portion
being the property of John Ballard and
W. H. Martin in joint partnership. For
many years Nevills has managed the
mine, dividing his residence between
Sonora, which is not many miles dis
tant from the Rawhide, and this city.
The latest change, as the story goes, is
that Nevills has been forced out of the
management by the combination of the
other two mineowners and the mine
has been placed in the hands of Sydney
Sprout, the builder of the electric plant
which supplies the operating power of
the Rawhide.
Ballard and Martin have been on the
ground for the past week in the ab
sence of Captain Nevills, who is now in
San Francisco. Captain Nevills said
last night that his men were in control
of the mine, and while he had not been
apprised of his displacement in favor of
Sprout, yet he admitted that he knew
that this project was on foot.
"There will be some big surprises in
a few days," he said last night. "My
attorneys, W. H. Ricketts and Thomas
Riordan, are now preparing suits which j
will be filed this week, and I can tell
yo-u they are full of big sensations.
These men think they have forced me j
out of it, but they will find that they
are mistaken. I will visit the mine in
a few days and look after matters my
self."
Attorney Riordan, when seen last
night, said that he was at work on le
gal papers which would be ready for
filing by Saturday and which would as
tonish Mr. Nevills' copartners and
others. He would not divulge the na
ture of the suits.
Rumors that the control of these
mines was to be taken' from Nevills
have been frequent, but nothing de
veloped until the bountiful rains of the
last few weeks gave the owners a
chance to resume work. Monday work
began in the Rawhide, and the ab
sence of Nevills after the long shut
down gave rise to considerable gossip.
Messrs. Ballard and Martin have been
on the ground for over a week, but they
kept their own counsel, until now it is
said to have been officially given out
that Captain Nevills will no longer su
perintend the work at the mines. Com
mon report has it that Ballard and
Martin have long been dissatisfied with
Nevills' actions, and they finally de
cided to take the reins in their own
hands.
This radical and sudden change has,
it appears, created more than ordin
ary excitement among the miners of
Tuolumne County, and they all wonder
what will happen next. The arrival of
Captain Nevills at Sonora is awaited
with suppressed excitement. Captain
Nevills has had absolute control of the
company interests for over nine years.
Some time after the decision in Boston,
which was adverse to the Eastern
claimants, who sought to purchase the
Rawhide, the owners of the mine got
together and organized a new corpora
tion. Before that the Rawhide was in
corporated, but the App was worked
under a partnership. Under the new
deal the properties were consolidated
and Ballard and Martin held two
thirds of the stock and Nevills held
one-third.
The Rawhide is one of the richest
mines' in the world, and Captain Nev
ills, who has long been considered the
moving spirit in its development, Is
known to every miner in the West.
To prevent any surpirse constables
are said to have been placed on duty at
the Rawhide and App mines.
"PLUTO" CLAIMS HIS
TRAINERS ROBBED HIM
NEW YORK, Jan. 18.â "Young
Pluto," the Australian prize fighter,
who was beaten here last night by
George Dixon, appeared in the Police
Court to-day and was granted sum
mons ifor his trainers. "Pluto," or Wil
liam Dudley Brown, as the young col
ored prize fighter is known in private
life, alleges that William Akers and
William Shannon, his trainers, both of
San Francisco, had appropriated his
share of last night's purse, $325. Fur
ther, Pluto said that when he asked his
trainers for the money they knocked
him down, beat him, kicked* him and
finally threw him out of the room.
Tom O'Rourke, manager of the Lenox
Athletic Club, where "Pluto" had pre
viously been pounded by Dixon, told
Pluto that his money had been handed
over to the trainers.
Pluto later appearedd at police head
quarters and asked that a detective be
sent with him to serve the summons.
Pluto said he was afraid of his train
ers. A detective was sent with him.
RUSSIANS MAY TAKE
POSSESSION OF HERAT
LONDON. Jan. 19.â The St. Peters
burg correspondent of the Daily Mall
says: In the event of disturbances fol
lowing the death of the Ameer of
Afghanistan, the Russian general,
Beguljuboff, Governor of the Trans-
Caspian district, who has a force of
20,000 men, has direct instructions to
take Herat, 360 miles west of Cabul. and
a post of great military importance.
ACT BARRING ALIENS
HAS BECOME A LAW
VICTORIA, B. C, Jan. 18.â The act
to prohibit aliens from owning placer
mines in British Columbia finally
passed the provincial Legislature this
afternoon and received the formal as
sent of the Lieutenant Governor. The
act goes into effect at once.
